Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
12 min
The Hanafi Definition of Fiqh: Subsidiary Shariah Wisdom
Defining fiqh and excluding muqallid from the definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three qualifiers of the Hanafi definition of fiqh (hikmah, far'iyyah, shar'iyyah) and apply the definition to distinguish a faqih from a muqallid.

Core Idea
Fiqh is confirmatory knowledge derived from shariah evidences with istinbat power.

This passage unpacks the technical Hanafi definition of fiqh. The matn states: 'Fiqh is a subsidiary, shariah-based wisdom.' The commentator clarifies that fiqh requires the ability to derive rulings (istinbat), which excludes the muqallid. Understanding this definition is foundational to Hanafi usul, as it sets the epistemological threshold for being a faqih.

Guided Path
1
Identify the matn definition
Locate the matn: 'wa al-fiqh hikmatun far'iyyatun shar'iyyatun.' These three qualifiers are the core of the Hanafi definition.
Matn is from Muslim al-Thubut.
2
Analyze 'hikmah'
Understand that hikmah here means confirmatory knowledge of shariah (halal and haram), as supported by Quranic verses and Ibn Abbas' interpretation.
Hikmah is not just any wisdom but specifically shariah knowledge.
3
Analyze 'far'iyyah'
Recognize that fiqh is subsidiary to usul al-fiqh and the principles of kalam; it is derived from higher-level principles.
Far'iyyah indicates fiqh is not independent but built upon foundational sciences.
4
Analyze 'shar'iyyah'
Fiqh must be based on shariah evidence (dalil shar'i) that leads to certainty.
Shar'iyyah ensures the rulings are rooted in divine revelation.
5
Apply to exclude the muqallid
The muqallid knows rulings but lacks istinbat power; thus, he does not possess 'hikmah' in the technical sense and is not called a faqih.
This distinction is unique to the Hanafi school.

شرع لغت میں منہج اور طریقہ کو کہتے ہیں اور اصطلاح میں اس طریقہ خہ س کو کہتے ہیں جس کو اللہ تعالیٰ نے اپنے بندوں کے لئے دین میں مشروع فرمایا۔ معنی یہ ہوا کہ فقہ ایسی حکمت فرعیہ کا نام ہے جو ایسی دلیل شرعی سے ثابت ہے جو صدق وصواب کے ساتھ ناطق ہے۔
قوله : فلا يقال على المقلد لتقصيره عن الطاقة . یہ عبارت تعریف سابق میں مذکور " لفظ حکمت پر متفرع ہے کہ تعریف فقہ کو حکمت کی قید سے مقید کر دینے کے سبب فقہ سے فقہ مقلد اور گروہ فقہاء سے مقلد خارج ہو گئے چونکہ حکمت اس علم قوی یا علم جازم کو کہتے ہیں جو دلائل سے ثابت ہو، یعنی دلائل شرعیہ سے قوت استنباط کے بعد جو علم حاصل ہوتا ہے حکمت کہلاتا ہے غرض حکمت کے مفہوم میں قوت استنباط ملحوظ و معتبر ہے اور مقلدین قوت استنباط سے عاری و خالی ہوتے ہیں۔ لہذا
It is needed just as in philosophy there is need for knowledge of the quality of production; rather, in fiqh and usul, and indeed in all sciences and arts, the need for it is equal. For example, in the aforementioned qiyas, the minor premise is the detailed evidence of fiqh, and the major premise is taken from usul al-fiqh, but the quality of production and arrangement of premises is from logic. Therefore, logic has an equal relation to every field; it is not that logic has a special relation only to philosophy, like the relation of usul al-fiqh to fiqh, and to the remaining sciences as an instrument, as the Banarsi scholar mistakenly thought.
Matn al-Muslim: "And fiqh is a subsidiary, shariah-based wisdom, and it is not applied to the muqallid due to his incapacity for ability, and the specification by sensory things is to avoid tasawwuf, an innovation. Yes, avoidance of kalam is a well-known custom."
Translation: And fiqh is called that confirmatory knowledge which is derived from the knowledge of essence and attributes or the knowledge of usul al-fiqh and its principles (kalam), and which is established by shariah evidences. And this definition does not apply to the muqallid because he lacks the power of istinbat. However, restricting the definition of fiqh to sensory things is an innovation to avoid tasawwuf. Yes, avoiding kalam is a well-known custom.
Explanation: After finishing the definition of the first part of the relative definition, i.e., the word 'usul', the author began the definition of the second part of the relative compound, i.e., fiqh, and said: "Fiqh is a subsidiary wisdom, etc." His statement: "al-fiqh" – There is disagreement in the definition of fiqh. Imam [al-Razi] in al-Mahsul and al-Muntakhab said: 'It is understanding the intent of the speaker from his speech.' Shaykh Abu al-Taliq in Sharh al-Luma' said: 'It is understanding precise things.' Al-Mahalli in Sharh Jam' al-Jawami' said: 'Fiqh linguistically means understanding.' The linguistic meaning of fiqh is understanding. Imam al-Isnawi considered this definition correct; al-Jawhari adopted it; and al-Amidi also relied on it. And it has also been said that fiqh is knowledge, but the truth is that understanding is contrary to knowledge; every scholar is not necessarily a profound understander, but every profound understander is not necessarily a scholar. His statement: "hikmah" – It can mean beneficial knowledge, knowledge of shariah, or confirmatory knowledge, but taking it as knowledge of shariah is obvious because in the noble Quran in most places hikmah means knowledge of shariah. Allah Most High says: "Whoever is given hikmah has been given much good." It is narrated from Abdullah ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with them both) that in the Quran hikmah means knowledge of halal and haram. Similarly, in the verse "Call to the way of your Lord with wisdom and good advice," hikmah means fiqh and shariah. So the author's intended meaning is that in shariah custom, hikmah itself is called fiqh because it is generally beneficial for creation. Since fiqh's being hikmah is among the specific characteristics of fiqh in shariah custom, as passed in the interpretation of Ibn Abbas. Therefore, in the author's statement "al-fiqh hikmatun far'iyyah," there are two possibilities: one is that this is the author's own definition of fiqh, as is apparent from the context; then later the definition of fiqh is mentioned with the third person plural "qalu" and attributed to the people, which also supports that this is the author's personal definition. The second possibility is that this is not the definition of fiqh but rather a mention of the characteristic of fiqh at the beginning so that the characteristic of complete distinction and perfect insight may be produced for the definition mentioned later. His statement "far'iyyah" means subsidiary, i.e., that which is derived from knowledge of essence and attributes, necessities of religion, principles of religion, or knowledge of usul al-fiqh and its principles, the science of kalam. His statement "shar'iyyah" – In language, shari' means path and way, and in terminology, it is the path that Allah has legislated for His servants in religion. The meaning is that fiqh is the name for that subsidiary wisdom which is established by a shariah evidence that speaks with truth and correctness.
His statement: "so it is not said of the muqallid due to his incapacity for ability." This statement is derived from the word 'hikmah' mentioned in the previous definition because restricting the definition of fiqh to hikmah causes the muqallid faqih and from the group of fuqaha' the muqallid to be excluded. Since hikmah is called that strong or certain knowledge which is established by evidences, i.e., the knowledge that results from the power of istinbat from shariah evidences is called hikmah. In short, in the meaning of hikmah, the power of istinbat is considered and recognized, and the muqallids are devoid and empty of the power of istinbat. Therefore...
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the definition of fiqh from the matn 'Muslim al-Thubut'. The commentator begins by clarifying that logic is needed across all sciences, not just philosophy, correcting a misconception. Then he presents the matn itself: 'Fiqh is a subsidiary, shariah-based wisdom; it is not applied to the muqallid due to his incapacity for ability; the specification by sensory things is to avoid tasawwuf, an innovation; yes, avoidance of kalam is a well-known custom.' The commentator then translates and elaborates.

He explains that fiqh is confirmatory knowledge derived from the knowledge of divine essence and attributes or from usul al-fiqh and its principles (kalam), established by shariah evidences. This definition excludes the muqallid because he lacks the power of istinbat (deriving rulings). The sensory restriction in the definition is an innovation aimed at distinguishing fiqh from tasawwuf, while the exclusion of kalam is a well-established custom.

The commentator then delves into the term 'hikmah' (wisdom), citing various scholars' definitions and Quranic verses to show that in shariah custom, hikmah refers to knowledge of shariah (halal and haram). He notes two possibilities: either the author himself is defining fiqh as 'subsidiary wisdom', or he is first describing a characteristic of fiqh to prepare for a later definition. The subsidiary nature means fiqh is derived from higher principles (like usul al-din and usul al-fiqh). The shariah nature means it is based on shariah evidence.

Finally, he explains the exclusion of the muqallid: because 'hikmah' implies strong knowledge derived from istinbat, which the muqallid lacks. Therefore, the definition of fiqh as hikmah automatically excludes the muqallid from being called a faqih (jurist) in the technical sense.

Key Terms
الفقه (al-fiqh): Confirmatory knowledge derived from shariah evidences, subsidiary to usul al-fiqh and kalam, requiring the power of istinbat.
الحكمة (al-hikmah): In this passage, hikmah is used to mean knowledge of shariah (halal and haram), as derived from Quran and interpretation of Ibn Abbas.
المقلد (al-muqallid): One who follows a mujtahid without knowing the evidence or having the ability to derive rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the definition of fiqh not applicable to the muqallid according to the Hanafi definition in this passage?
A) Because he does not know the rulings
B) Because he lacks the power of istinbat (derivation)
C) Because he follows a different school
D) Because his knowledge is not confirmatory

Key Takeaway
Fiqh, in the Hanafi technical sense, is confirmatory knowledge requiring istinbat power; thus, a muqallid is not considered a faqih.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
6 min
Why a Muqallid Is Not a Faqih: The Condition of Istinbat
Explaining why a muqallid is not called a faqih

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the Hanafi technical term 'faqih' and identify why a muqallid is excluded from this designation based on the condition of quwwat al-istinbat.

Core Idea
A faqih is not just one who knows rulings, but one who can derive them from evidence; a muqallid lacks this ability.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, the term 'faqih' carries a precise technical meaning that goes beyond mere knowledge of rulings. It requires the capacity for istinbat — extracting rulings directly from the primary sources. This lesson unpacks the definition from the commentary on Muslim al-Thubut, showing how the condition of 'hikmah' in the definition of fiqh necessarily excludes the muqallid (blind follower). We will also examine the scriptural proofs that support this restriction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key term: hikmah
The passage states that fiqh is defined as 'hikmah', which means knowledge established by proofs that requires the strength of derivation. Understand that hikmah in this context implies active reasoning from evidence.
Hikmah is not just wisdom but knowledge built on dalil.
2
Understand the exclusion of the muqallid
A muqallid lacks quwwat al-istinbat; he accepts rulings without knowing their evidences or derivation. Therefore, he does not possess the hikmah required for being a faqih, and the label 'faqih' does not apply to him.
This is a definitional exclusion, not a judgment on piety.
3
Examine the scriptural evidence
The author cites Quran 9:122 and several hadith that describe a faqih as one who gains deep understanding (tafaqquh) in religion. These texts imply active learning and derivation, not mere imitation.
The evidence supports that a faqih must be able to engage with the sources.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : فلا يقال على المقلد لتقصيره عن الطاقة . یہ عبارت تعریف سابق میں مذکور " لفظ حکمت پر متفرع ہے کہ تعریف فقہ کو حکمت کی قید سے مقید کر دینے کے سبب فقہ سے فقہ مقلد اور گروہ فقہاء سے مقلد خارج ہو گئے چونکہ حکمت اس علم قوی یا علم جازم کو کہتے ہیں جو دلائل سے ثابت ہو، یعنی دلائل شرعیہ سے قوت استنباط کے بعد جو علم حاصل ہوتا ہے حکمت کہلاتا ہے غرض حکمت کے مفہوم میں قوت استنباط ملحوظ و معتبر ہے اور مقلدین قوت استنباط سے عاری و خالی ہوتے ہیں۔ لہذا قرآن و حدیث میں جو فقیہ کی تعریف بیان کی گئی ہے مثلا فلولا نفر من كل فرقة طائفة ليتفقهوا في الدين الآية. خياركم في الجاهلية خياركم فى الاسلام اذا فقهوا الحديث اذا اراد الله بعبد خيرا يفقهه في الدين الحدیث وغیر ہا مقلد پر صادق نہیں آتی ۔
His statement: 'So it is not said of the muqallid because of his falling short of the capacity.' This phrase branches off from the word 'hikmah' mentioned earlier in the definition. Because the definition of fiqh is restricted by the condition of 'hikmah', the muqallid and the group of muqallids among the fuqaha are excluded from fiqh. Since 'hikmah' is called that strong knowledge or definitive knowledge that is established by proofs, i.e., the knowledge that is obtained after the strength of derivation (istinbat) from Shariah evidences. In short, in the meaning of hikmah, the strength of derivation is considered and taken into account, and muqallids are devoid of and empty of the strength of derivation. Therefore, the definition of faqih mentioned in the Qur'an and Hadith, for example, 'Why should not a group from every faction go forth to gain understanding in religion' (9:122), and 'The best of you in Jahiliyyah are the best of you in Islam when they gain understanding,' and 'When Allah desires good for a servant, He gives him understanding in religion,' and other such texts do not apply to the muqallid.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage clarifies why a muqallid (one who blindly follows without evidence) is not called a faqih in the technical sense. The author's definition of fiqh as 'hikmah' requires that the knowledge be established through proofs and the ability to derive (istinbat). A muqallid lacks this capacity because he merely follows without understanding the evidence or how to derive rulings. The commentator supports this by citing Qur'anic verses and hadith that define a faqih as one who gains deep understanding and ability to derive rulings. Thus, the title faqih is reserved for those who can engage in ijtihad, even if on a limited scale, not for mere followers.

Key Terms
مقلد (muqallid): One who follows a mujtahid without knowing the evidence or deriving rulings himself.
قوت استنباط (quwwat al-istinbat): The ability to extract rulings from primary sources using juristic reasoning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why is a muqallid not called a faqih?
A) Because he doesn't practice what he knows
B) Because he lacks the strength of derivation (quwwat al-istinbat)
C) Because he only knows the rulings of fiqh
D) Because he is not a Muslim

Key Takeaway
A muqallid is not a faqih because the definition of fiqh requires the ability to derive rulings from evidence (quwwat al-istinbat).

Definition of Fiqh by Later Scholars and Objections
Defining fiqh and defending against objections of comprehensiveness and exclusion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi definition of fiqh from the later Shafi'i definition, identify the two objections raised against the Shafi'i definition, and explain the Hanafi responses using the concepts of malaka and amarat.

Core Idea
Fiqh is a malaka (faculty) in the mujtahid, not mere knowledge in a muqallid.

In this lesson, we examine a critical passage from Ashraf al-Nu'ut where the author discusses the definition of fiqh given by later scholars, primarily Shafi'i usulists. The definition 'knowledge of shar'i rulings from their detailed evidences' seems straightforward, but it faces two serious objections: it fails to cover all rulings (since even faqihs are sometimes ignorant) and it might include muqallids. The Hanafi responses refine the meaning of 'knowledge' and 'evidences' to preserve fiqh as a specialized faculty exclusive to mujtahids.

Guided Path
1
Parse the definition
Identify the two components of the definition: 'knowledge' and 'detailed evidences'. Note that this definition is attributed to later (Shafi'i) scholars.
The definition is 'knowledge of shar'i rulings from their detailed evidences.'
2
Identify the two objections
First objection: if 'all rulings' are meant, the definition is not comprehensive because faqihs may not know some rulings (la adri). Second objection: if 'absolute rulings' are meant, a knowledgeable muqallid would be included.
These are the two classic objections to this definition.
3
Analyze the Hanafi responses
First response: 'knowledge' means malaka (faculty), so the possibility of not knowing some rulings does not negate the faculty. Second response: 'evidences' mean indicators (amarat) that produce conjecture; the obligation to act on conjecture is exclusive to the mujtahid, excluding the muqallid.
The muqallid's basis is the statement of the mujtahid, not his own conjecture.
4
Understand the implication
The muqallid is not a faqih because he does not have the malaka and does not rely on amarat. The author warns against equating the mujtahid's and muqallid's positions.
The practical consequence: only mujtahids are faqihs in the Hanafi school.

ترجمه : اور متاخرین نے فقہ کی تعریف یوں کی ہے احکام شرعیہ کو اس کے ادلہ تفصیلیہ سے جاننا۔ اس تعریف پر اعتراض کیا گیا ہے کہ فقہ کی تعریف میں مذکور احکام سے اگر جمیع احکام مراد ہیں تو تعریف جامع نہیں کیونکہ فقہاء سے لا ادری ثابت ہے یا مطلق احکام مراد ہیں تو تعریف دخول غیر سے مانع نہیں رہی کیونکہ مقلد عالم اس وقت اس میں داخل ہو رہا ہے۔ اور اعتراض کا یہ جواب دیا گیا کہ احکام سے جمیع احکام مراد ہیں پھر بھی لا ادری تعریف کے لئے مضر نہیں کیونکہ تعریف فقہ میں علم سے مراد ملکہ ہے لہذا تخلف ممکن ہے اور یہ بھی جواب دیا گیا ہے کہ ادلہ سے امارات مراد ہے (امارات ظن کو کہتے ہیں ) اور وجوب عمل کے علم کا حاصل کرنا بواسطہ طن اجمالی طور پر مجتہد کا خاصہ ہے رہا مقلد تو اس کا مستند اس کے مجتہد کا قول ہوتا ہے اس کے حق میں مقلد کاظن نہ ظن مجتہد معتبر ہے اس لئے فرق سمجھوتا کہ ان لوگوں کی طرح تم بھی نہ کہنے لگو کہ جس طرح مجتہد کا مظنون مجتہد کے لئے واجب اعمل ہوتا ہے اسی طرح اس کے مقلد کے لئے بھی ہوتا ہے کہ وہ دونوں کے دونوں برابر ہیں۔ البتہ اس وقت لازم آئے گا کہ فقہ وجوب العمل بالا حکام کے علم سے عبارت ہے وہ علم بالا حکام کا نام نہیں ہے مگر یہ کہا جائے کہ رسم ہے لہذا الازم سے تعریف جائز ہے اور اس میں بھی وہ ہے جو ہے اور یہیں سے تمکو اس اعتراض کے اندفاع کا علم ہو گیا ہو گا جو اس مقام پر کیا جاتا ہے، کہ فقہ باب خنیات سے ہے تو اس پر علم کا اطلاق کیسے ہو گا ؟ علاوہ ازیں علم کا اطلاق در حقیقت غیر تصور پر بھی ہوتا ہے۔

And that by 'evidences' is meant indicators, and attaining knowledge of the obligation of action through conjecture is exclusively for the mujtahid by consensus. As for the muqallid, his basis is the statement of his mujtahid, not his own conjecture, nor the conjecture of his mujtahid? So understand the difference, lest you say like someone said: 'Just as the conjecture of the mujtahid is binding upon him, so too upon his muqallid, so they are equal.' Yes, it would necessitate that fiqh is knowledge of the obligation to act by rulings, not knowledge of the rulings themselves, unless it is said that it is a definition (rasm), so it is permissible by implication, but there is something in that. And from here you know the repulsion of what was said: 'Fiqh is from the category of conjectures, so how can it be knowledge?' While in reality knowledge applies to what is not conceptualization. Also. Translation: And the later scholars defined fiqh as: knowing the shar'i rulings from their detailed evidences. This definition has been objected to: if by 'rulings' all rulings are meant, then the definition is not comprehensive because it is established that jurists are ignorant of some rulings; or if absolute rulings are meant, then the definition does not prevent the entry of others because the knowledgeable muqallid would enter into it. The answer given is: by 'rulings' all rulings are meant, yet the ignorance (of some rulings) does not harm the definition because in the definition of fiqh, 'knowledge' means a faculty (malaka), so failure is possible. And also it is answered that by 'evidences' is meant indicators (which are conjectural), and attaining knowledge of the obligation of action through conjecture in a general way is a characteristic of the mujtahid exclusively. As for the muqallid, his basis is the statement of his mujtahid; for him, his own conjecture nor the conjecture of the mujtahid is valid. So understand the difference, so that you do not become like those who say: 'Just as the conjecture of the mujtahid is obligatory to act upon for him, so too for his muqallid, they are equal.' On the contrary, it would necessitate that fiqh is expressive of knowledge of the obligation to act upon rulings, not knowledge of the rulings themselves. But if it is said that it is a drawing (rasm), then it is permissible by implication, and there is something in that. And from here you will know the repulsion of the objection raised at this place: that fiqh is from the door of conjectures, so how can it be called knowledge? Moreover, knowledge in reality applies also to non-conceptualization. Elaboration: When the author finished explaining the characteristic of fiqh, he started this definition which the people mentioned. Therefore he used the plural 'they defined.' The plural pronoun refers either to the people or to most Shafi'is, because this definition is transmitted from most Shafi'is. Thus Imam al-Razi in al-Mahsul, al-Armawi in al-Hasil, the author of al-Tahsil in al-Tahsil, al-Subki in Jam' al-Jawami', Qadi al-Baydawi in Minhaj al-Usul, and Ibn al-Hajib in Mukhtasar al-Usul defined fiqh in this way.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the definition of fiqh as given by later scholars (متأخرون), primarily Shafi'i usul scholars, and the objections raised against it from a Hanafi perspective. The author begins by quoting the matn which addresses the objection that fiqh is from the realm of conjecture and cannot be called knowledge. The response is that knowledge here means a faculty (malaka) to derive rulings, and that the mujtahid's conjecture is binding on him, while the muqallid relies on the mujtahid's statement. The Urdu translation clarifies the definition: 'knowing the shar'i rulings from their detailed evidences.' Two objections are raised: if 'all rulings' are meant, the definition is not comprehensive because jurists are sometimes ignorant (لا ادری); if 'absolute rulings' are meant, then a knowledgeable muqallid would be included. Two answers are given: first, that 'knowledge' in the definition is a malaka, so failure to know some rulings is possible without negating the malaka; second, that 'evidences' mean indicators (amarat) that produce conjecture, and the obligation to act is limited to the mujtahid, excluding the muqallid. The author warns against equating the mujtahid's and muqallid's positions. The passage then explains that the definition might be a rasm (descriptive definition) using implication, and closes by defending that fiqh can be called 'ilm even though it is based on conjecture, because 'ilm can apply to that which is not mere tasawwur but includes tasdiq. The elaboration (تشريح) identifies the source of this definition: it is from the Shafi'i school, listing several prominent Shafi'i usul works. The Hanafi position is not explicitly given here, but the discussion shows that the Hanafi definition of fiqh is different, emphasizing malaka and the mujtahid's role.

Key Terms
الفقه (al-fiqh): Knowledge of shar'i rulings from their detailed evidences, understood as a faculty (malaka) in the mujtahid.
الملکة (al-malaka): A firm faculty (firmly rooted ability) in the soul that enables the derivation of rulings.
الامارة (al-amara): An indicator that leads to conjecture (zann), as opposed to definitive evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response in this passage, why is a muqallid not included in the definition of fiqh?
A) Because the muqallid does not have knowledge of all rulings.
B) Because the muqallid's basis is his own conjecture.
C) Because the muqallid's basis is the statement of his mujtahid, not detailed evidences.
D) Because the muqallid has a malaka of fiqh but does not act upon it.

Key Takeaway
Fiqh is a malaka (faculty) for deriving rulings from indicators, not mere knowledge, and is therefore exclusive to mujtahids.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

The Four Meanings of Hukm in the Definition of Fiqh
Breaking down the definition of fiqh into components and listing four meanings of 'hukm'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four meanings of hukm as discussed in Hanafi usul and determine which meaning is operative in the definition of fiqh, explaining why the other meanings are excluded.

Core Idea
Hukm has four meanings in usul; only one — the ascription of a judgment from detailed evidences — correctly defines fiqh.

The definition of fiqh as 'knowledge of the shar'i rulings derived from their detailed evidences' hinges on the precise meaning of 'hukm'. In this passage, the commentator unpacks the word 'ahkam' by listing four possible interpretations. Only one fits the definition without circularity or overextension, ensuring that fiqh remains a discipline rooted in detailed proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the components of the definition
The definition of fiqh is 'al-ilm bi-l-ahkam al-shar'iyya min adillatiha al-tafsiliyya'. Break it down: 'knowledge' (ilm), 'shar'i rulings' (ahkam shar'iyya), and 'from detailed evidences' (min adillatiha al-tafsiliyya). Each term requires careful interpretation.
The pronoun in 'that it' refers back to fiqh.
2
Understand 'knowledge' as tasdiq
The commentator specifies that 'ilm' here means tasdiq (affirmation/judgment), not mere tasawwur (conception). This includes both certain (yaqini) and probable (zanni) judgments. Fiqh is thus a set of beliefs about rulings, not just concepts.
Tasdiq is a key term in usul: it is the act of affirming a proposition.
3
Enumerate the four meanings of hukm
The author lists four meanings: (1) the address of Allah (the text); (2) the legal effect (e.g., obligation, prohibition); (3) the ascription of a judgment to an action (positive or negative); (4) perception that a relation exists or not. List them clearly and understand the distinctions.
Each meaning leads to a different interpretation of 'fiqh is knowledge of rulings'.
4
Evaluate and select the correct meaning
The commentator rejects the first because it reduces fiqh to knowing that a text is Allah's address, not the ruling itself. The second is plausible but narrow (only effects). The fourth is rejected because it leads to a tautology (knowledge of knowledge). The third meaning is correct because it involves ascription of a judgment derived from detailed evidences, avoiding circularity and matching the full definition.
The qualifiers 'shar'iyya' and 'min adillatiha al-tafsiliyya' further refine the meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله بانه العلم بالاحكام الشرعية الخ
اس کے قول بانہ کی ضمیر متصل فقہ کی طرف راجع ہے اور علم سے تصدیق بالمسائل مراد ہے یعنی جو تصور نہ ہولہذا تصدیق یقینی و تصدیق ظنی دونوں کو شامل رہے گا اس کا قول احکام حکم کی جمع ہے حکم کا اطلاق کئی معنوں پر ہوتا ہے (۱) الحکم خطاب الله تعالى المتعلق بافعال المكلفين بالاقتضاء او التخيير ، اس تقدیر پر معنی یہ ہو گا الفقه العلم بان هذا خطاب الله تعالى ( ۲ ) هو الحكم الشرعى اى الاثر المترتب على خطاب اللہ تعالیٰ اور اثر خطاب وجوب، حرمت ، ندب را باحت و کراہت ہیں پس اس تقدیر پر معنی یہ ہوگا کہ فقہ وجوب و حرمت و غیرها کے جاننے کا نام ہے (۳) الحکم اسناد امرالی امر آخر ایجاباً وسلباً ۔ اس معنی کے اعتبار سے معنی یہ ہوگا کہ فقہ قضا یا شرعیہ کی تصدیق کا نام ہے جو ادلہ تفصیلیہ سے حاصل ہو ( ۴ ) الحکم ادراک ان النسبة واقعۃ اولیست بواقعہ یعنی حکم وقوع نسبت بالا وقوع نسبت کے ادراک کا نام ہے۔ یہ معنی مراد نہیں ہوسکتا کیونکہ ادراک علم سے عبارت ہے اس تقدیر پر اس کے قول اعلم بالا حکام الشرعیۃ کا معنی العلم بالا دراكات الشرعیہ ہو جائے گا اور ادراکات چونکہ علوم ہوتے ہیں تو اب یہ مطلب ہوگا۔ الفقہ العلم بالعلوم الشرعیہ یعنی فقہ علوم شرعیہ کے علم کا نام ہے اور یہ ظاہر ہے کہ فقہ علوم شرعیہ کے علم کا نام نہیں ہے بلکہ عین علوم شرعیہ کا نام ہے۔ اس کا قول شرعیہ کی قید سے احکام عقلیہ جیسے العالم حادث اور حسیہ جیسے النار حرقہ اور دیگر وہ تمام احکام خارج ہو گئے جو شرح سے ماخوذ نہیں ہے۔ عن ادلتها التفصيلية، العلم سے متعلق ہے معنی یہ ہوگا کہ فقہ ان احکام شرعیہ کے تصدیق کو کہتے ہیں جو حاصل ہو ان دلیلوں سے جو ہر ہر حکم کے ساتھ مخصوص ہے اس قید سے علم الہی خارج ہو گئے کہ علم الہ احتیاج علت سے پاک ہے وہ علیم و خبیر احکام و ادلہ دونوں کو خوب جانتا ہے کیوں کہ اس کو جس طرح یہ معلوم ہے کہ
His statement: 'that it is knowledge of shar'i rulings, etc.' The pronoun in 'that it' refers back to fiqh. By 'knowledge' is meant tasdiq (affirmation) of issues, i.e., not concept (tasawwur). So it includes both certain tasdiq and conjectural tasdiq. His statement 'rulings' is the plural of 'hukm'. Hukm is applied in several meanings: (1) Hukm is the address of Allah the Exalted related to the actions of the responsible (mukallaf) by demand or option. On this interpretation, the meaning would be: fiqh is knowledge that this is the address of Allah. (2) It is the shar'i hukm, i.e., the effect resulting from the address of Allah, and the effect of the address is obligation, prohibition, recommendation, permissibility, and dislike. So on this interpretation, the meaning would be: fiqh is the name for knowing obligation, prohibition, etc. (3) Hukm is the attribution of one thing to another in affirmation or negation. According to this meaning, it would be: fiqh is the name for the affirmation of shar'i judgments which are obtained from detailed evidences. (4) Hukm is perception that a relation actually exists or does not exist, i.e., it is the name for perception of the occurrence of a relation or its non-occurrence. This meaning cannot be intended because perception is synonymous with knowledge; on this interpretation, his statement 'knowledge of shar'i rulings' would mean knowledge of shar'i perceptions, and since perceptions are knowledges, the meaning would be: fiqh is knowledge of shar'i knowledges, i.e., fiqh is the name for knowledge of shar'i sciences. And it is obvious that fiqh is not the name for knowledge of shar'i sciences, but rather it is the name for the very shar'i sciences. His statement 'shar'i' excludes rational rulings like 'the world is originated' and sensory rulings like 'fire burns' and all other rulings that are not derived from revelation. 'From their detailed evidences' is related to 'knowledge'. The meaning would be: fiqh is the affirmation of those shar'i rulings that are obtained from those evidences that are specific to each ruling. This excludes divine knowledge, because the divine knowledge is free from need of cause; He is All-Knowing and All-Aware, He knows both rulings and evidences well, just as He knows that... (incomplete)
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage is an analytical breakdown of the phrase 'al-ʿilm bi-l-aḥkām al-sharʿiyya min adillatihā al-tafṣīliyya' from the definition of fiqh. The author (the commentator) explains each component. First, 'knowledge' here means tasdiq (affirmation/judgment) rather than mere tasawwur (conception), so it covers both certain and probable tasdiq. Then he discusses the word 'aḥkām' (plural of ḥukm) and lists four possible meanings of ḥukm: (1) the address of Allah (the text of the Quran or Sunnah) – this would make fiqh knowledge that a certain text is Allah's address; (2) the sharʿī effect of that address (e.g., obligation, prohibition) – fiqh would then be knowledge of these effects; (3) the ascription of a sharʿī judgment (positive or negative) – fiqh would be the affirmation of such judgments derived from detailed evidences; (4) perception that a relation exists or not – but this is rejected because it would lead to fiqh being 'knowledge of knowledge' (a tautology). The author states that the third meaning is the correct one for the definition, as it properly requires derivation from detailed evidences. He also explains the qualifier 'sharʿiyya' excludes rational and sensory judgments. The phrase 'min adillatihā al-tafṣīliyya' excludes divine knowledge (Allah's knowledge) because Allah does not need evidences. The passage ends abruptly, but it continues the explanation of the exclusion of divine knowledge.

Key Terms
الحكم (al-hukm): Has four possible meanings: (1) Allah's address; (2) legal effect; (3) ascription of a judgment; (4) perception of a relation. The third is preferred for defining fiqh.
التصديق (al-tasdiq): Affirmation or judgment (as opposed to pure conception), includes both certain and probable knowledge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which meaning of 'hukm' does the commentator consider correct for the definition of fiqh?
A) The address of Allah the Exalted.
B) The legal effect (obligation, prohibition).
C) The ascription of a shar'i judgment in affirmation or negation.
D) Perception of the existence or non-existence of a relation.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the correct meaning of 'hukm' for defining fiqh is the ascription of a shar'i judgment derived from detailed evidences, not the mere text or legal effect.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

The Classical Definition of Fiqh: Unpacking Each Component
Expansion and analysis of the classical definition of fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the four components of the classical definition of fiqh and identify which types of knowledge are excluded by each component.

Core Idea
Fiqh is not just any religious knowledge—it is knowledge of shar'i rulings derived specifically from detailed evidences.

The definition of fiqh as 'knowledge of the shar'i rulings from their detailed evidences' is the foundational starting point of Hanafi usul al-fiqh. This passage unpacks each part of that definition, clarifying what counts as knowledge, what a 'ruling' means, what 'shar'i' excludes, and what 'detailed evidences' demands. Understanding these exclusions is crucial for distinguishing fiqh from taqlid, theology, and other Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Four Key Terms
The definition consists of four elements: 'ilm (knowledge), 'ahkam (rulings), 'shar'iyyah (shar'i), and 'an adillatiha al-tafṣiliyyah (from their detailed evidences). Each term carries specific legal and epistemological weight.
Note the plural 'adillah' indicates multiple types of evidence.
2
Understand 'Ilm as Tasdiq
In this context, 'ilm means taṣdīq (affirmation) of the issues, not mere tasawwur (conceptualization). It includes both certain (yaqini) and probable (zanni) knowledge. This broadens the scope of fiqh to cover rulings established through ahad hadith or qiyas.
Hanafi usul distinguishes between 'ilm (certain) and zann, but here 'ilm is used loosely.
3
Analyze the Meanings of Hukm
The plural 'ahkam' is derived from 'hukm', which has four possible meanings: (1) Allah's address requiring or allowing, (2) the effect of that address (obligation, prohibition, etc.), (3) attribution of one thing to another, (4) perception of a relation. The commentator rules out the fourth because it leads to tautology.
The preferred meanings for fiqh are (2) and (3), aligning with the standard understanding.
4
Apply the Exclusion of 'Shar'iyyah'
The restriction 'shar'iyyah' excludes rational rulings (e.g., 'the world is created') and sensory rulings (e.g., 'fire burns') as well as any rulings not derived from Islamic sources.
This ensures fiqh deals exclusively with divine commands.
5
Apply the Exclusion of 'An Adillatiha al-Tafṣiliyyah'
This phrase excludes knowledge that is not derived from detailed evidences. It removes divine knowledge (Allah knows without evidence), prophetic knowledge (knowledge with evidence but not derived from it), angelic knowledge (intuitive), and the knowledge of a muqallid (who relies on general evidence like 'my mujtahid said so').
Only mujtahids truly possess fiqh; muqallids have taqlid, not fiqh.

کیا جو قوم نے بیان کیا ہے۔ اس لئے وعرفوہ صیغہ جمع غائب استعمال کیا۔ ضمیر جمع یا تو قوم کی طرف راجع ہے یا اس سے اکثر شافعیہ مراد ہیں کیونکہ اس طرح تعریف اکثر شافعیہ سے منقول ہے چنانچہ امام نے محصول میں، ارموی نے حاصل میں ، صاحب التحصیل نے ”التحصیل اور سبکی نے جمع الجوامع میں، قاضی بیضاوی نے منہاج الاصول میں اور علامہ ابن حاجب نے مختصر الاصول میں اسی طرح فقہ کی تعریف کی ہے۔ قوله بانه العلم بالاحكام الشرعية الخ اس کے قول بانہ کی ضمیر متصل فقہ کی طرف راجع ہے اور علم سے تصدیق بالمسائل مراد ہے یعنی جو تصور نہ ہولہذا تصدیق یقینی و تصدیق ظنی دونوں کو شامل رہے گا اس کا قول احکام حکم کی جمع ہے حکم کا اطلاق کئی معنوں پر ہوتا ہے (۱) الحکم خطاب الله تعالى المتعلق بافعال المكلفين بالاقتضاء او التخيير ، اس تقدیر پر معنی یہ ہو گا الفقه العلم بان هذا خطاب الله تعالى ( ۲ ) هو الحكم الشرعى اى الاثر المترتب على خطاب اللہ تعالیٰ اور اثر خطاب وجوب، حرمت ، ندب را باحت و کراہت ہیں پس اس تقدیر پر معنی یہ ہوگا کہ فقہ وجوب و حرمت و غیرها کے جاننے کا نام ہے (۳) الحکم اسناد امرالی امر آخر ایجاباً وسلباً ۔ اس معنی کے اعتبار سے معنی یہ ہوگا کہ فقہ قضا یا شرعیہ کی تصدیق کا نام ہے جو ادلہ تفصیلیہ سے حاصل ہو ( ۴ ) الحکم ادراک ان النسبة واقعۃ اولیست بواقعہ یعنی حکم وقوع نسبت بالا وقوع نسبت کے ادراک کا نام ہے۔ یہ معنی مراد نہیں ہوسکتا کیونکہ ادراک علم سے عبارت ہے اس تقدیر پر اس کے قول اعلم بالا حکام الشرعیۃ کا معنی العلم بالا دراكات الشرعیہ ہو جائے گا اور ادراکات چونکہ علوم ہوتے ہیں تو اب یہ مطلب ہوگا۔ الفقہ العلم بالعلوم الشرعیہ یعنی فقہ علوم شرعیہ کے علم کا نام ہے اور یہ ظاہر ہے کہ فقہ علوم شرعیہ کے علم کا نام نہیں ہے بلکہ عین علوم شرعیہ کا نام ہے۔ اس کا قول شرعیہ کی قید سے احکام عقلیہ جیسے العالم حادث اور حسیہ جیسے النار حرقہ اور دیگر وہ تمام احکام خارج ہو گئے جو شرح سے ماخوذ نہیں ہے۔ عن ادلتها التفصيلية، العلم سے متعلق ہے معنی یہ ہوگا کہ فقہ ان احکام شرعیہ کے تصدیق کو کہتے ہیں جو حاصل ہو ان دلیلوں سے جو ہر ہر حکم کے ساتھ مخصوص ہے اس قید سے علم الہی خارج ہو گئے کہ علم الہ احتیاج علت سے پاک ہے وہ علیم و خبیر احکام و ادلہ دونوں کو خوب جانتا ہے کیوں کہ اس کو جس طرح یہ معلوم ہے کہ میں نے بندوں پر نماز کو واجب قرار دیا ہے اسی طرح بندوں پر وجوب نماز کا بھی علم کا بھی علم بغیر کسی دلیل کے ہے کہ وہ ایجاب وجوب دونوں کو جانتا ہے۔ یونہی اس قید سے علم النبی اور علم جبرئیل علیہ علیھما السلام بھی خارج ہو گئے کیونکہ ان دونوں کا علم بھی ادلہ کے ساتھ ہے ادلہ سے نہیں ہے دوسری وجہ یہ ہے کہ ان دونوں کا علم علم بدیہی ہے اور علم بدیہی ادلہ کے ساتھ ہوتا ہے ادلہ کے بعد نہیں ہوتا۔ ادلہ تفصیلیہ کی قید سے علم مقلد بھی خارج ہو گیا کہ ادلہ سے ادلہ اربعہ قرآن وحدیث اور اجماع و قیاس مراد ہیں اور ان سے مجتہد ہی کو علم حاصل ہوتا ہے رہے مقلد تو وہ مسائل کو دلیل اجمالی سے جانتا ہے مثلاً وہ یوں کہے گا ۔ هذا الحكم افتی به مجتهدي - وكل مايفتى به المجتهد فهو حكم الله تعالى فهذا الحكم من الله تعالى اعتراض : بہت سارے مصنفین جیسے صاحب تنقیح وغیرہ نے فقہ کی تعریف میں لفظ شرعیہ کو عملیہ سے مقید کر دیا ہے مصنف نے ایسا کیوں نہیں کیا حالانکہ یہ تعریف اس قوم سے مروی ہے؟ جواب :۔ پہلے گذرا کہ فقہ میں تصوف و اخلاق داخل ہے جن لوگوں نے عملیہ کی قید لگا کر اسے فقہ سے خارج کر دیا ہے وہ اس زمانہ کی بدعت ہے متقدمین کے یہاں فقہ وتصوف میں چولی دامن کا ساتھ رہا چونکہ مصنف کے یہاں طریقہ اسلاف پر عمل کرنا اولی وانسب ہے طریقہ اخلاف پر عمل کرنے کے یہ نسبت ، اس لئے عملیہ کی قید نہیں لگائی تا کہ احتر از تصوف کا وہم جاتا رہے۔
What the people (qawm) have stated. Therefore, they used the plural verb 'wa 'arrafūhu'. The plural pronoun either refers to the people, or to the majority of the Shafi'is, because this definition is transmitted from the majority of the Shafi'is. Thus, Imam al-Mahsūl, al-Armawi, Sāhib al-Tahṣīl, Subkī in Jam' al-Jawāmi', Qāḍī Bayḍāwī in Minhāj al-Uṣūl, and 'Allāmah Ibn Ḥājib in Mukhtaṣar al-Uṣūl all defined fiqh similarly. His statement 'annahu al-'ilm bi al-aḥkām al-shar'iyyah etc. — The pronoun in 'annahu' refers to fiqh. By 'ilm is meant taṣdīq bi al-masā'il (affirmation of the issues), not mere tasawwur. Thus, it includes both certain and conjectural taṣdīq. His statement 'aḥkām' is the plural of 'ḥukm'. The term ḥukm is used in several meanings: (1) al-ḥukm is the address of Allah the Exalted related to the acts of the morally responsible, requiring or allowing. On this interpretation, the meaning is: fiqh is knowledge that this is an address of Allah. (2) It is the shar'i ruling, i.e., the effect resulting from the address of Allah. The effects of the address are obligation, prohibition, recommendation, permission, and dislike. So on this interpretation, the meaning is: fiqh is knowing obligation, prohibition, etc. (3) al-ḥukm is the attribution of one thing to another, affirmatively or negatively. On this meaning, the meaning is: fiqh is the affirmation of shar'i rulings obtained from detailed evidences. (4) al-ḥukm is the perception that a relation is real or not real. This meaning cannot be intended because perception is synonymous with knowledge; on this interpretation, the phrase 'al-ilm bi al-aḥkām al-shar'iyyah' would mean 'knowledge of shar'i perceptions', and perceptions are knowledges, so it would mean 'fiqh is knowledge of shar'i knowledges', which is incorrect because fiqh is not knowledge of shar'i knowledges but rather the shar'i knowledges themselves. His restriction 'shar'iyyah' excludes rational rulings like 'the world is created' and sensory rulings like 'fire burns', and all rulings not derived from the Sharia. 'an adillatihā al-tafṣīliyyah' is related to 'al-ilm'; the meaning is: fiqh is the affirmation of those shar'i rulings which are obtained from the evidences that are specific to each ruling. This restriction excludes divine knowledge, because divine knowledge is free from need for cause; He knows both rulings and evidences well, for just as He knows that He has made prayer obligatory on servants, so too does He know the obligation of prayer without any evidence, as He knows both the act of obligating and the obligation. Similarly, this restriction excludes the knowledge of the Prophet and the knowledge of Jibrīl, because their knowledge is with evidence, not from evidence; secondly, their knowledge is intuitive, and intuitive knowledge occurs with evidence, not after evidence. The restriction of detailed evidences also excludes the knowledge of a muqallid, because by evidences are meant the four: Quran, Hadith, Ijmā', and Qiyās, and only a mujtahid obtains knowledge from them. As for the muqallid, he knows issues through a general evidence, e.g., he will say: 'This ruling is a fatwa of my mujtahid, and whatever the mujtahid gives fatwa on is the ruling of Allah, so this ruling is from Allah.' Objection: Many authors like the author of al-Tanqīḥ have restricted the term 'shar'iyyah' with 'amaliyyah' in the definition of fiqh. Why did the author not do so, even though this definition is transmitted from that group? Answer: It has passed earlier that fiqh includes tasawwuf and akhlaq. Those who have added the restriction of 'amaliyyah' and excluded it from fiqh have introduced an innovation of this time. Among the early scholars, fiqh and tasawwuf went hand in hand. Since the author's way is to follow the predecessors, which is more appropriate and preferable than following the successors, he did not add the restriction of 'amaliyyah', so that the suspicion of avoiding tasawwuf is removed.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the definition of fiqh as 'knowledge of the shar'i rulings from their detailed evidences'. The commentator begins by noting that this definition is transmitted primarily from the Shafi'i school, mentioning several prominent Shafi'i usul works. He then unpacks each component of the definition. First, 'ilm (knowledge) here means tasdiq (affirmation) of the issues, not mere tasawwur (conceptualization), so it includes both certain and probable affirmations. Second, the term 'ahkam' (pl. of hukm) is analyzed into four possible meanings: (1) Allah's address concerning the acts of the mukallaf; (2) the shar'i effect (e.g., obligation, prohibition); (3) attribution of one thing to another; and (4) perception of a relation. The commentator rules out the fourth because it would lead to a tautology. His preferred interpretation is the second or third, which align with the standard Hanafi understanding that fiqh deals with practical rulings (ahkam amaliyyah). Third, the restriction 'shar'iyyah' excludes rational and sensory rulings. Fourth, the phrase 'an adillatiha al-tafṣiliyyah' (from their detailed evidences) excludes the knowledge of Allah, the Prophet, and Jibril (whose knowledge is intuitive or with evidence not from it), and also the knowledge of a muqallid (who relies on minimal evidence). The passage then addresses an objection: why did the author not restrict the definition with 'amaliyyah' (practical)? The answer is that the early scholars included tasawwuf and akhlaq within fiqh; restricting it to practical rulings is a later innovation. Thus, the author preserves the classical comprehensive definition.

Key Terms
حكم (ḥukm): In this context, the shar'i ruling or the divine address related to the acts of the mukallaf, having multiple possible meanings as detailed in the text.
علم (ʿilm): Knowledge in the sense of tasdiq (affirmation of issues), including both certain and conjectural knowledge.
شرعية (sharʿiyyah): Relating to the Sharia; used to exclude rational and sensory rulings, as well as those not derived from Islamic sources.
ادلة تفصيلية (adillah tafṣīliyyah): Detailed evidences, i.e., the specific proofs for each individual ruling, as opposed to general evidence (dalīl ijmālī).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, the phrase 'an adillatiha al-tafṣiliyyah' excludes which of the following?
A) The knowledge of a muqallid
B) The knowledge of a mujtahid
C) The knowledge of a scholar of hadith
D) The knowledge of a student of fiqh

Key Takeaway
Fiqh is knowledge of shar'i rulings derived specifically from the detailed evidences of the Quran, Sunnah, Ijma, and Qiyas, excluding taqlid and intuitive knowledge.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
advanced
8 min
Definition of Fiqh: Comprehensiveness and Exclusiveness Objections and Response
Analyzing definition of fiqh with two objections and the answer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two objections to the standard definition of fiqh, explain why they arise, and articulate the Hanafi response that 'ilm means malaka.

Core Idea
Fiqh is not knowing every ruling but possessing the faculty (malaka) to derive them.

The definition of fiqh as 'knowledge of the Shari'ah rulings' seems straightforward, but it immediately raises two critical objections: it may exclude the greatest mujtahids and include the muqallid. This passage from Ashraf al-Nu'ut walks us through these objections and the classical Hanafi resolution, which shifts the meaning of 'knowledge' to a faculty (malaka) of derivation. Understanding this debate is essential for grasping the nature of ijtihad and the distinction between a mujtahid and a muqallid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition under scrutiny
Locate the standard definition being critiqued: fiqh is knowledge of the Shari'ah rulings. Although not explicitly quoted, it is the starting point of both objections.
This definition is attributed to the scholars in the text.
2
Analyze the first objection: comprehensiveness
Understand that if the article 'al' in 'al-ahkam' is istighraq (generality) or jins (genus), then 'knowledge of all rulings' is required. This would exclude even the greatest mujtahids like Abu Hanifa and Malik, who admittedly did not know every ruling individually.
Examples: Abu Hanifa said 'I do not know' on some issues; Malik did not know thirty-six out of forty questions.
3
Analyze the second objection: exclusiveness
If the article 'al' is taken as 'ahd (specific reference) and specifically mental 'ahd (absolute reference), then 'knowledge of rulings' would include any knowledge of Shari'ah rulings, even if few. This would mean a muqallid who knows some rulings would be a faqih, which contradicts consensus.
The verse 'I fear that the wolf may eat him' is cited as an example of absolute reference.
4
Examine the response: 'ilm as malaka
The author presents the standard Hanafi response: 'ilm in the definition does not mean actual knowledge but rather malaka, a firmly rooted faculty of deriving rulings. Thus, a mujtahid possesses this malaka even if he does not know every ruling, and a muqallid lacks it even if he knows some rulings.
The response is attributed to Ibn Hajib and Mahalli, but criticized by Sadr al-Sharia and Taftazani.
5
Evaluate the response's strength
Note that the author uses the passive voice ('it is answered') to hint at the weakness of this response. Consider whether the shift from knowledge to malaka fully resolves both objections or if further refinement is needed.
This passage sets the stage for deeper usul discussions on the definition of fiqh.

قوله اوردان كان المراد الجميع فلا ينعكس الخ
تعریف مذکور مشہور پر ( دواعتراضات میں سے ایک ضرور وارد ہے ) مصنف نے اس عبارت میں دونوں اعتراضات کا ذکر کیا ہے۔
اعتراض اول : ۔ یہ ہے کہ تعریف جامع نہیں کہ اس سے ان فقہاء کرام کا فقہ خارج ہورہا ہے جنکی فقاہت آفتاب نصف النہار سے زیادہ روشن ہے۔ وہ اسطرح کہ تعریف میں مذکور لفظ الا حکام پر الف لام یا تو استغراق کا ہے یا جنس کا ہے بہر دو تقدیر جمیع احکام کے جاننے پر فقہ اور جمیع احکام کے جاننے والے ہی پر فقیہ کا اطلاق ہو گا جو جمیع احکام نہ جانے گا ان کے علم کو فقہ اور خود اس کو فقیہ نہیں کہا جا سکتا کہ الاحکام کا الف و لام استغراق و جنسی کلیت کا متقاضی ہے جب کہ امام اعظم جیسے فقیہ اعظم اور امام مالک جیسے ماہر فقہیات سے بعض مسائل کا عدم علم ثابت ہے چنانچہ دھر کے بارے میں حضرت امام اعظم سے پوچھا گیا تو فرمایا اداری حضرت امام مالک سے چالیس مسائل دریافت کئے گئے تو چھتیں مسائل کے بارے میں لا ادری فر مایا ۔" حاصل اعتراض یہ ہے کہ اگر احکام سے جمیع احکام مراد ہوں تو امام اعظم ابو حنیفہ اور امام مالک جیسے فقہاء کا علم فقہ سے خارج ہو جائے گا جبکہ ان دونوں کا فقیہ ہو نا متفق علیہ اور اجماعی ہے۔ اعتراض دوم : ۔ یہ ہے کہ تعریف دخول غیر سے مانع نہیں کہ اس میں مقلد عالم کا علم داخل ہورہا ہے وہ اس طرح کہ تعریف مذکور میں ذکر کردہ لفظ الا حکام پر الف لام امیرا داول کے سبب استغراق و جنس کا نہیں ہوسکتا تو لامحالہ الف لام عبد کا ہوگا پھر عبد خارجی کا نہیں ہو سکتا اس لئے کہ بعض معین و شخص مراد لینے پر کوئی دلیل نہیں ہے پس عبد ذہنی کا ہونا متعین ہو گیا جو مطلق کی طرف دال ہے جیسے اخاف أن ياكله الذئب میں ہے اور مطلق قلیل و کثیر پر دلالت کرتا ہے پس احکام تقلیل ہوں یا ترسب پر الا حکام کا اطلاق درست ہو گا جب احکام سے بعض قلیل و کثیر سب مراد ہو سکتے ہیں تو اس تقدیر پر وہ مقلد عالم جو بعض احکام شرعیہ کو دلیل سے جانتا ہے اس کے علم کا فقہ ہونا اور خود اس کا فقیہ ہونا لازم آیا حالانکہ مقلد عالم کا علم اجماعا فقہ نہیں اور وہ فقیہ نہیں کہلاتا۔ حاصل یہ کہ تعریف قوم جامع و مانع نہیں ہے۔ قوله اجيب بانه لايضر لا ادرى لان المراد الملكة الخ
اس عبارت سے اعتراض اول کے جواب کی طرف اشارہ ہے اور مجہول کا صیغہ اس لئے استعمال کیا تاکہ جواب کے ضعف کی طرف بھی اشارہ ہو جائے علامہ ابن حاجب نے مختصر میں یہی جواب دیا ہے اور محلی نے شرح جمع الجوامع میں اس کو پسندیدہ جواب قرار دیا ہے لیکن صدر الشریعہ نے توضیح میں اور تفتازانی نے تلویح میں اس جواب پر تعقب کیا ہے مصنف نے ان دونوں کو راش پر چلتے ہوئے صیغہ مجہول استعمال کیا۔
جواب کا خلاصہ :- یہ ہے کہ تعریف میں الا حکام سے جمیع احکام ہی مراد ہیں پھر بھی اس کی جامعیت پر لا ادری سے اعتراض وارد نہ ہوگا کیونکہ تعریف میں علم سے مراد ملکہ ہے اور ملکہ جمیع احکام شرعیہ کے استنباط کی کیفیت راسخہ کا نام ہے جب علم سے مراد ملکہ ہے تو عبارت کا مطلب یہ ہوگا کہ فقہ
His statement: 'If the general meaning is intended, then it is not convertible etc.' There are two objections to the mentioned famous definition. The author mentioned both objections in this phrase. First objection: The definition is not comprehensive because it excludes the fiqh of those great jurists whose juristic ability is brighter than the midday sun. This is because the article 'al' in 'al-ahkam' in the definition is either for generality or for the genus; in either case, fiqh is applied to knowing all rulings, and faqih is applied only to the one who knows all rulings. For those who do not know all rulings, their knowledge cannot be called fiqh and they cannot be called faqih, because 'al' necessitates generality and totality. Yet it is established that great jurists like Imam A'zam and Imam Malik did not know some rulings. For example, Imam A'zam was asked about [something] and he said 'I do not know'; Imam Malik was asked forty questions and he said 'I do not know' for [thirty-six] of them. The conclusion of the objection is that if 'ahkam' means all rulings, then the knowledge of jurists like Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Malik would be excluded from fiqh, whereas their being jurists is agreed upon by consensus. Second objection: The definition is not exclusive because it includes the knowledge of a knowledgeable muqallid. This is because if the 'al' in 'al-ahkam' cannot be for generality or genus due to the first objection, then it must be for 'ahd'; it cannot be for external 'ahd because there is no evidence for specifying a particular meaning, so it must be for mental 'ahd, which indicates absolute generality, like in the verse 'I fear that the wolf may eat him'. This absolute includes both few and many. So whether the rulings are few or many, the term 'al-ahkam' applies. On this basis, the knowledge of a muqallid who knows some rulings from evidence would be fiqh and he would be a faqih, whereas the knowledge of a muqallid is not fiqh by consensus and he is not called a faqih. So the definition of the scholars is neither comprehensive nor exclusive. His statement: 'It is answered that 'I do not know' does not harm because what is meant is malaka etc.' This phrase indicates the answer to the first objection. The passive voice is used to indicate the weakness of the answer. Allama Ibn Hajib gave this answer in his Mukhtasar, and Mahalli in his Sharh Jam' al-Jawami' considered it a good answer. But Sadr al-Sharia in al-Tawdih and Taftazani in al-Talwih criticized it. The author followed both, using the passive voice. Summary of the answer: The definition means all rulings by 'al-ahkam', yet the objection based on 'I do not know' does not harm because 'ilm in the definition means malaka, and malaka is a firmly rooted quality for deriving all Shari'ah rulings. When 'ilm means malaka, the meaning of the phrase is that fiqh is...
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the definition of fiqh, presenting two objections to the standard definition (that fiqh is knowledge of the Shari'ah rulings) and the response. The first objection is that the definition is not comprehensive: if 'al-ahkam' means all rulings, then even the greatest mujtahids like Abu Hanifa and Malik who admittedly did not know every ruling would be excluded. The second objection is that the definition is not exclusive: if the article 'al' is taken as for 'ahd (a specific set), it could be mental 'ahd, meaning absolute, which would include the knowledge of a muqallid who knows only some rulings, but a muqallid is not a faqih by consensus. The response is that 'ilm in the definition is not actual knowledge but malaka, i.e., the faculty of deriving rulings. Thus, a mujtahid has the malaka even if he does not know each ruling individually, and a muqallid lacks this faculty. The author notes that the answer is given in the passive voice to acknowledge its weakness, as later scholars like Sadr al-Sharia and Taftazani criticized it. Nonetheless, it is the accepted response in Hanafi usul.

Key Terms
فقہ (fiqh): Knowledge of the Shari'ah rulings, specifically the faculty (malaka) of deriving them
ملکہ (malaka): A firmly rooted quality in the soul enabling derivation of rulings
مقلد (muqallid): One who follows a mujtahid without knowledge of the detailed evidence
استغراق (istighraq): The article 'al' indicating all individuals of the category

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the response to the first objection, what does 'ilm in the definition of fiqh refer to?
A) Knowledge of all rulings individually
B) The malaka (faculty of derivation)
C) Knowledge of some rulings
D) Consensus of scholars

Key Takeaway
Fiqh is the malaka (faculty) to derive Shari'ah rulings, not mere knowledge of all rulings.

Responding to Two Objections on the Definition of Fiqh
Defends the definition of fiqh against two objections by clarifying key terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi definition of fiqh as 'malaka' (faculty) for deriving all ahkam does not exclude imams who said 'I don't know' and why it excludes a muqallid's knowledge, by applying the concepts of malaka and amarat.

Core Idea
Fiqh is a faculty for deriving all rulings, not actual knowledge of every rule — thus an imam's 'I don't know' does not negate his fiqh.

In this passage, the author defends the definition of fiqh as 'knowledge of all shari rulings from their detailed proofs' against two objections. The first objection points to great imams like Abu Hanifa and Malik who sometimes admitted ignorance. The second objection warns that a learned muqallid who knows some rulings with proof would be included. The author responds by clarifying that 'knowledge' here means a firmly rooted faculty (malaka), not actual knowledge, and that the 'proofs' refer to certain indicators (amarat) that only a mujtahid can fully engage with. We will unpack these arguments step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two objections
Locate the two criticisms aimed at the definition of fiqh: (1) the 'I don't know' objection from the practice of imams, and (2) the inclusion of a muqallid who knows some rulings.
Both objections challenge the definition's comprehensiveness and exclusivity.
2
Analyze the first response: 'ilm as malaka
The author responds that 'ilm in the definition means malaka (faculty), not actual knowledge. This allows a faqih to have the faculty for all rulings even if obstacles prevent actual knowledge of some rulings.
Malaka is a firmly rooted quality (kayfiyya rasikha) enabling derivation.
3
Analyze the second response: 'adilla as amarat
The author responds that 'adilla in the definition means amarat (indicators that lead to certain knowledge of the obligation to act). Such certainty is only attained by a mujtahid, not a muqallid.
Amarat produce yaqin (certainty) of wujub al-'amal, distinguishing mujtahid from muqallid.
4
Consider the critique and rebuttal
The passage includes a critique from Sharh al-Tanqih about the nature of the 'tahiyya' (quality). The author rebuts that it is the ready power of derivation, not proximate or remote quality.
This further clarifies what malaka encompasses.
5
Synthesize the definition
Conclude that the definition of fiqh as 'knowledge of all ahkam from their adilla' is both comprehensive (includes mujtahids with malaka) and exclusive (excludes muqallids) when 'ilm and 'adilla are properly interpreted.
The definition stands against both objections after these clarifications.

Just as ignorance of some issues is established for expert jurists. For example, about the (dhahr?) Imam Abu Hanifa was asked and he said 'I do not know'. Imam Malik was asked forty issues and he said 'I do not know' for thirty issues. The gist of the objection: if by 'ahkam' is meant all rulings, then the knowledge of jurists like Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Malik would be excluded from fiqh, while their being jurists is agreed upon and consensual. The second objection: that the definition does not prevent the inclusion of others, because it includes the knowledge of a learned muqallid. That is: in the mentioned definition, the definite article 'al' on 'al-ahkam' cannot be for complete inclusion or genus due to the ruling of ['amir dawil'?], so inevitably it is the 'al' of mental reference, which indicates absoluteness, like in 'I fear that the wolf may eat him'. And the absolute indicates both few and many. So whether the rulings are few or many, the term 'al-ahkam' applies. On this basis, the learned muqallid who knows some shari rulings with proof - his knowledge would be fiqh and he would be a jurist, whereas the knowledge of a muqallid is not fiqh by consensus and he is not called a jurist. So the definition of the people (of usul) is neither comprehensive nor exclusive. 'His statement: it was answered that 'I do not know' does not harm because what is meant is the faculty.' This phrase points to the answer to the first objection, and the passive voice is used to indicate the weakness of the answer. Allama Ibn Hajib gave this answer in al-Mukhtasar, and al-Mahalli considered it a favored answer in Sharh Jam' al-Jawami', but Sadr al-Shari'a in al-Tawdih and Taftazani in al-Talwih objected to this answer. The author used the passive voice following both. Summary of the answer: in the definition, by 'ahkam' all rulings are indeed intended, yet the objection based on 'I do not know' does not harm comprehensiveness because by 'knowledge' in the definition is meant 'malaka' (faculty), and malaka is the name for a firmly rooted quality of deriving all shari rulings. When 'ilm' means malaka, the meaning of the phrase is: fiqh is the name for a firmly rooted quality of extracting all shari rulings. It is not the meaning that actual knowledge of all shari rulings is called fiqh. Therefore, it is possible that at times when there are obstacles and attachments, like when proofs conflict, or there is conflict between imagination and intellect, or due to resemblance of truth and falsehood, or when the mujtahid's nature is afflicted with a disease of mix-up, actual knowledge of some rulings may not exist, but when obstacles are removed, that knowledge is attained through the faculty of derivation and ready power. The gist is: by 'ahkam' all rulings are intended, but for being fiqh and a faqih, actual knowledge of all rulings is not necessary; rather, having the malaka of deriving all rulings is sufficient, and here by 'ilm' malaka is meant, so the lack of actual knowledge of some rulings does not contradict malaka. Refutation by Sharh al-Tanqih: Sharh al-Tanqih and others objected to this answer, saying that by 'tahiyy' (quality?) is meant either proximate or remote. If proximate quality is meant, it is not well-defined; if remote quality is meant, then it is also possessed by the non-faqih muqallid. Answer to the refutation: by 'tahiyya' is not meant proximate or remote, but rather it is the name for that ready power through which derivation and extraction of all rulings is possible, and for the faqih this much is sufficient. 'And his statement: and that by 'adilla' is meant 'amarat' (indications).' This phrase answers the second objection. The gist: the knowledge of the muqallid would enter the definition if 'ilm bi al-ahkam' meant certain knowledge or general knowledge, and 'adilla' meant the four proofs and the statement of the mujtahid, but that is not the case. Rather, by 'ilm bi al-ahkam' is meant certain knowledge of rulings, and by 'adilla' is meant 'amarat', which is the art that leads to knowledge of the obligation to act upon rulings. And certain knowledge of the obligation to act upon rulings through rational proofs and indications is obtained by consensus only and solely by the mujtahid. The muqallid does not obtain certain knowledge from conjecture and indications, because there is consensus that acting according to his conjecture is obligatory for the mujtahid. So when the mujtahid obtains conjecture about a ruling via indications, it becomes obligatory for him to act upon it. Unlike the conjecture of the muqallid, because his conjecture is not knowledge.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is a discussion within the Hanafi usul tradition about the definition of fiqh (jurisprudence). The author presents two objections to the definition of fiqh as 'knowledge of the shari rulings from their detailed proofs' and provides responses. The first objection: if fiqh is knowledge of all rulings, then great imams like Abu Hanifa and Malik who sometimes said 'I don't know' would not be faqihs. The response: 'ilm (knowledge) in the definition refers to malaka (a firmly rooted faculty), not actual knowledge of every ruling. A faqih may not know a particular ruling due to obstacles, but his faculty of derivation remains. The second objection: the definition would include a learned muqallid who knows some rulings with proof. The response: the 'knowledge' intended is certain knowledge (yaqini) obtained from the 'adilla' which here means 'amarat' (indicators that lead to certainty of obligation to act). Such certainty is only obtained by the mujtahid, not the muqallid. The muqallid's knowledge is based on conjecture, not certainty. Thus the definition remains exclusive to mujtahids. The author also notes a further critique from Sharh al-Tanqih and its rebuttal.

Key Terms
الفقه (al-fiqh): A firmly rooted faculty (malaka) for deriving all shari rulings from their detailed proofs.
الملكة (al-malaka): A firmly rooted quality (kayfiyya rasikha) enabling derivation of rulings.
الإمارات (al-amarat): Indications that lead to certain knowledge of the obligation to act (wujub al-'amal) upon rulings.
المقلد (al-muqallid): One who follows a mujtahid without knowing the proofs or possessing the faculty of derivation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the term 'amarat' refer to in the definition of fiqh?
A) The four primary proofs (Quran, Sunnah, Ijma, Qiyas).
B) Indicators that lead to certain knowledge of the obligation to act.
C) Conjectural evidence used by muqallids.
D) The consensus of the Companions.

Key Takeaway
Fiqh in the Hanafi school is a faculty for deriving all rulings; a faqih need not know every ruling actually, and a muqallid's knowledge is not fiqh because it lacks certain knowledge from amarat.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate

Definition of Fiqh as Malakah and Exclusion of the Muqallid
Defining fiqh as a malakah and excluding the muqallid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define fiqh as a malakah according to Hanafi usul and explain why a muqallid is not considered a faqih.

Core Idea
Fiqh is a faculty of derivation, not mere knowledge; a muqallid lacks this faculty.

In this passage, the author defends the Hanafi definition of fiqh against objections, clarifying that fiqh is a deeply rooted faculty (malakah) for deriving rulings, not simply knowing them. He then uses consensus to distinguish between the mujtahid, for whom his own speculation is binding, and the muqallid, who merely follows his mujtahid's statement. Understanding this distinction is essential for grasping the boundaries of ijtihad and taqlid in Hanafi thought.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the definition of fiqh as malakah
Recognize that the author defines fiqh as a firmly rooted quality for deriving all shar'i rulings, not actual knowledge. Understand the role of obstacles and the concept of preparedness (tahiyyah).
The key is that malakah is sufficient even if some rulings are not actually known at the moment.
2
Analyze the first objection and response
Examine the critique from Sharh al-Tanqih regarding 'readiness' (near or far) and the author's reply that tahiyyah is the power of derivation, not a specific state.
The author rejects the dichotomy of near/far readiness.
3
Identify the meaning of 'evidences' (adillah) as amarat
See that the author reinterprets 'evidences' to mean indicators (amarat) that produce certain knowledge of obligation only for the mujtahid.
This excludes the muqallid from the definition.
4
Examine the doubt about the muqallid's knowledge
Understand the potential confusion that a muqallid also seems to have certain knowledge, and the author's response that his basis is the mujtahid's statement, not speculation or detailed evidences.
The mujtahid's statement is not a detailed evidence.
5
Contrast the binding force of speculation for mujtahid vs muqallid
Review the consensus that the mujtahid's speculation binds him, but not the muqallid, and the refutation of Amanullah Banarasi who equated them.
Two consensuses are cited: one for mujtahid, one for muqallid.

جمیع احکام شرعیہ کے استخراج کی کیفیت راسخہ کا نام ہے یہ معنی نہیں ہے کہ جمیع احکام شرعیہ کے علم بالفعل کا نام فقہ ہے لہذا ایسا ہوسکتا ہے کہ موانع و علائق جیسے ادلہ کے معارض ہونے کے وقت ، وہم و عقل کے مابین معارضہ ہونے کے سبب حق و باطل میں مشاکلت کے باعث یا طبیعت مجتہد میں مرض ترج لاحق ہونے کی وقت بعض احکام کا علم بالفعل نہ ہو سکے لیکن جب موانع دور ہو جاتے ہیں تو ملکہ استنباط اور استعدادی قوت سے وہ علم حاصل ہو جاتا ہے۔
حاصل جواب یہ ہے کہ احکام سے جمیع احکام مراد ہیں مگر فقہ وفقیہ ہونے کے لئے جمیع احکام کا علم بالفعل ہونا ضروری نہیں ہے بلکہ جمیع احکام کے استنباط کے ملکہ کا ہونا کافی ہے اور یہاں علم سے ملکہ ہی مراد ہے لہذا بعض احکام کے علم بالفعل کا متخلف ہونا ملکہ کے منافی نہیں ۔
شرح تنقیح کا تعقب : شرح تنقیح وغیرہ نے اس جواب پر تعقب کرتے ہوئے کہا ہے کہ نہیں سے تہید قریب مراد ہے یا تہو بعید۔ اگر تہو قریب مراد ہے تو یہ غیر منضبط ہے اور اگر تہیو بعید مراد ہے تو یہ غیر فقیہ مقلد کو بھی حاصل ہے۔
جواب تعقب : تہیہ سے مراد قریب و بعید نہیں ہے بلکہ تمہیں اس قوت استعدادی کا نام ہے جس کے ذریعہ جمیع احکام کا استنباط واستخراج ممکن ہو اور فقیہ کے لئے اتنا ہی کافی ہے۔
وقوله وبأن المراد بالادلة الامارات الخ
اس عبارت سے اعتراض دوم کا جواب دیا ہے۔
حاصل جواب یہ ہے کہ تعریف فقہ میں علم مقلد اس وقت داخل ہوتا جب علم بالا حکام علم یقینی علم ظمنی کو اور ادلہ، ادلہ اربعہ وقول مجتہد کو عام ہوتا حالانکہ ایسا نہیں ہے بلکہ علم بالا حکام سے احکام کا علم یقینی مراد ہے اور ادلہ سے امارات مراد ہے امارات اس فن کو کہتے ہیں جو وجوب العمل بالا حکام کے علم کی طرف مفضی ہو اور وجوب العمل بالا حکام کا علم یقینی خطن وامارات کے واسطہ سے بالا جماع صرف اور صرف مجتہد کو حاصل ہوتا ہے ظن و امارات سے مقلد کو علم یقینی نہیں حاصل ہوتا کیونکہ اسپر اجماع منعقد ہے کہ مجتہد پر اس کے ظن کے مطابق عمل واجب ہے پس جب مجتہد کو کسی حکم کے متعلق بواسطہ امارات ظن حاصل ہو جاتا ہے تو اس کو اسپر عمل کرنا واجب ہو جاتا ہے برخلاف ظن مقلد کے کہ اسکا ظن علم بالا حکام کا ذریعہ نہیں ہو سکتا اور نہ ہی مجتہد کاخن ہی اس کے حق میں معتبر ہے لہذا مقلد کسی بھی طرح تعریف میں داخل نہیں۔
قوله - اما المقلد فمستنده قول مجتهده الخ اس عبارت سے ایک وہم کا ازالہ کیا ہے یہاں وہم ہوسکتا ہے کہ مقلد کوبھی اپنے مجتہد کے ظن کے واسطہ سے علم یقینی ہوتا ہے تو آخر علم مجتہد و علم مقلد میں کیا فرق ہوا جبکہ دونوں کا علم علم یقینی ہی ہے؟
جـواب وهم : یہ ہے کہ وجوب العمل بالا حکام کے علم کے حصول میں مقلد کا مستند خود مقلد کاظن ہے نہ ہی اس کے مجتہد کا ظن ہے۔ یعنی مقلد کوحکم شرعی معلوم ہونے کی دلیل قول مجتہد ہے اس مقلد کا خود ذاتی ظن یا اس کے مجتہد کا ظن علم مقلد کے لئے کافی نہیں چنانچہ اگر مجتہد کو کسی حکم کاخن ہوا لیکن اس نے اپنے مقلد سے نہیں کہا تو مقلد پر عمل واجب نہیں ہوتا حالانکہ مجہتد پر اس کے خطن کے مطابق عمل واجب ہے۔ الغرض نہ ظن مجتہد ہی نہ ظن مقلد ہی مقلد کے حق میں نفع بخش ہے پس مقلد کا علم تعریف فقہ سے اس اعتبار سے بھی خارج ہو گیا کہ وجوب عمل کا علم مقلد کو نہ مجتہد کے ظن سے حاصل ہے نہ خود اس کے ظن سے بلکہ قول مجتہد سے ہے اور یہ ظاہر ہے کہ قول مجہتد ادلہ تفصیلیہ سے نہیں ہے جبکہ تعریف میں ادلہ تفصیلیہ مذکور ہے۔
قوله - فاعرف الفرق حتى لاتقل مثل من قال الخ
اس عبارت سے مصنف نے صاحب محکم الاصول جناب امان اللہ بنارسی پر رد کیا ہے علامہ بنارسی نے محکم میں کہا ہے " مظنون مجتہد وجوب عمل کے حق میں مجتہد و مقلد دونوں پر یکساں ہے پس جس طرح مظنون مجتہد، مجتہد کے لئے واجب العمل ہے اسی طرح اس کے مقلد کے لئے بھی واجب العمل ہے غرض دونوں میں اس اعتبار سے کوئی فرق نہیں ہے۔ مصنف کہتے ہیں یہ خیال فاسد ہے صحیح یہ ہے کہ دونوں میں فرق ہے وہ یہ ہے کہ مجتہد کا مظنون، خود مجتہد کے حق میں تو واجب العمل ہے لیکن مقلد کے حق میں واجب العمل نہیں ہے کیونکہ اس پر اجماع ہے کہ مجتہد کو اس کے ظن کے بموجب وجوب عمل کا جزم ہوتا ہے برخلاف مقلد کے کہ اس پر بھی اجماع ہے کہ مجتہد کاٹن یا مقلد کاظن مقلد کو وجوب عمل کے علم کا جزم نہیں کرتا ، بلکہ مقلد پر قول مجتہد کے بموجب عمل واجب ہوتا ہے حاصل یہ کہ وجوب عمل کے
The name of a firmly rooted quality of deriving all shar'i rulings. It is not meant that fiqh is the actual knowledge of all shar'i rulings. Hence it is possible that at times when there are obstacles and impediments, such as when evidences conflict, when there is conflict between intuition and reason, due to similarity between truth and falsehood, or when the nature of the mujtahid is affected by something, actual knowledge of some rulings may not be obtained. But when the obstacles are removed, that knowledge is obtained through the malakah (faculty) of istinbat and the power of preparedness. The essence of the answer is that by 'rulings' all rulings are meant, but for being a faqih, actual knowledge of all rulings is not necessary; rather, having the malakah to derive all rulings is sufficient. Here 'knowledge' means malakah. Therefore, the lack of actual knowledge of some rulings does not contradict the malakah. Critique of Sharh al-Tanqih: Sharh al-Tanqih and others criticized this answer saying that 'tahiyyah' (readiness) either means near readiness or far readiness. If near readiness, it is not well-defined; if far readiness, it is also possessed by a non-faqih muqallid. Response to critique: Tahiyyah does not mean near or far; rather it is the name of that preparatory power through which the derivation and extraction of all rulings is possible, and it is sufficient for a faqih. And his statement: 'And that the intended meaning of evidences is indicators (amarat).' With this phrase, he answers the second objection. The essence of the answer is: In the definition of fiqh, the knowledge of a muqallid would be included if 'knowledge of rulings' meant certain knowledge or speculative knowledge, and 'evidences' included the four sources and the mujtahid's opinion. But that is not the case. Rather, 'knowledge of rulings' means certain knowledge of rulings, and 'evidences' means indicators (amarat). Amarat is the term for that which leads to knowledge of the obligation to act upon rulings. And certain knowledge of the obligation to act upon rulings, through indicators, by consensus, is only acquired by the mujtahid. A muqallid does not acquire certain knowledge from indicators and speculation, because there is consensus that a mujtahid must act according to his speculation. So when a mujtahid obtains speculation about a ruling through indicators, acting upon it becomes obligatory for him, unlike the speculation of a muqallid, because his speculation cannot be a means to knowledge of the obligation to act, nor is the mujtahid's speculation valid for him. Therefore, a muqallid is in no way included in the definition. His statement: 'As for the muqallid, his basis is the statement of his mujtahid.' This phrase removes a doubt. Here a doubt could arise that a muqallid also obtains certain knowledge through his mujtahid's speculation, so what is the difference between the knowledge of the mujtahid and the knowledge of the muqallid, since both have certain knowledge? Answer to the doubt: In acquiring knowledge of the obligation to act upon rulings, the muqallid's basis is neither his own speculation nor his mujtahid's speculation. That is, the evidence for the muqallid's knowledge of a shar'i ruling is the statement of the mujtahid. Neither his own personal speculation nor his mujtahid's speculation is sufficient for the muqallid's knowledge. Thus, if a mujtahid has speculation about a ruling but does not tell his muqallid, the muqallid is not obligated to act, even though the mujtahid must act according to his speculation. In short, neither the mujtahid's speculation nor the muqallid's speculation is beneficial for the muqallid. Therefore, the muqallid's knowledge is excluded from the definition of fiqh on this basis: the muqallid's knowledge of the obligation to act is obtained neither from the mujtahid's speculation nor from his own speculation, but from the mujtahid's statement. And it is obvious that the mujtahid's statement is not among the detailed evidences, while the definition mentions detailed evidences. His statement: 'So know the difference so that you do not say like the one who said...' With this phrase, the author refutes Sahib Mahkam al-Usul, Janab Amanullah Banarasi. Allamah Banarasi said in Mahkam: 'The speculation of the mujtahid regarding the obligation to act is the same for both the mujtahid and the muqallid. Just as the mujtahid's speculation is binding on the mujtahid, similarly it is binding on his muqallid. In short, there is no difference between them in this respect.' The author says this is a corrupt notion. The correct view is that there is a difference: the mujtahid's speculation is binding on the mujtahid himself, but it is not binding on the muqallid, because there is consensus that the mujtahid is certain of the obligation to act based on his speculation, unlike the muqallid, regarding whom there is consensus that the mujtahid's speculation or the muqallid's speculation does not give the muqallid certainty of the obligation to act. Rather, the muqallid must act according to the mujtahid's statement. The essence is that of the obligation to act...
