قوله الانتاج مبنى على التثليث - الخ
گذرا کہ منطقی حضرات مفرد پر دلیل کا اطلاق نہیں کرتے ہیں بلکہ ان کے نزدیک دلیل ایسے دو قول کے مرکب کا نام ہے جس سے قول آخر لازم آئے ، اس سے ظاہر ہے کہ منطقی کے نزدیک دلیل میں ترکیب ضروری ہے اس کے برخلاف اصولی کے نزدیک دلیل میں ترکیب نہیں ہوتی مگر صرف اس حیثیت سے کہ نظر واستدلال کا تعلق ترکیب سے وابستہ ہے اس مفہوم کی طرف ماتن نے اپنے قول الانتاج سے اشارہ کیا ہے اسکا حاصل یہ ہے کہ دلیل مفرد کا نتیجہ دینا اور مطلوب تک پہونچانا دلیل کے مفرد ہونے کی حالت میں نہیں ہو سکتا بلکہ دلیل مفرد کے نتیجہ دینے کے لئے تین امور کا وجود ضروری ہے اور یہ اس لئے کہ دلیل مفرد بنفسہ مطلوب تک نہیں پہونچا سکتی ، لہذا ایک مطلوب کا موضوع ایک مطلوب کا محمول اور دونوں کے درمیان ایک واسطہ ہونا ضروری ہے جو ان دونوں کو ایک دوسرے سے مربوط کر دے، اس مفہوم کی طرف مصنف نے اذ لا بد من واسطہ ، سے اشارہ کیا ہے اور واسطہ کا مطلوب کے دونوں طرف یعنی محکوم علیہ اور محکوم بہ کے درمیان ہونا ضروری ہے ورنہ ذہن ثانی سے اول کی طرف منتقل نہ ہوگا کیونکہ اشیاء متبائنہ میں سے ایک سے دوسرے کی طرف توصل ممکن نہیں اس لئے ایک ایسا امر ضروری ہے جو دونوں کو مستلزم ہوتا کہ جب محکوم بہ کو اس کے لئے ثابت کیا جائے تو وہ محکوم علیہ کے لئے التزاماً ثابت ہو جائے تب ایسا ارتباط جو صحت انتاج ہے پایا جائے گا اس طرح دلیل کے منتج ہونے کے لئے دو مقدمے ضروری ہیں۔ مقدمہ اولی کو صغری اور مقدمہ ثانیہ کو کبری کہتے ہیں اور اسکے نتیجہ کے موضوع و محمول کو اصغر و کبر سے تعبیر کرتے ہیں چونکہ اول مطلوب کے موضوع اور واسطہ پر اور دوم
For example, by correct consideration in change and occurrence, the sought-after news (i.e., knowledge that the world has a creator) is obtained in such a way that some of the states of the world are predicated upon the world, and we say 'the world is changing', then by connecting its second state to the first, we say 'everything changing is originated', so from this the sought-after news (the world is originated) is obtained intuitively, which was not obtained before. Here it should be kept in mind that according to the usuliyyun, the subject of the sought-after (i.e., the minor term like 'world') is the proof, while according to the logicians both premises are proof. The author only mentioned 'world' in the example to indicate that in the terminology of the usuliyyun, only 'world' is the proof for proving the Creator. So by correct examination of its states, reaching the sought-after news is not merely possible but actual and established. In short, in the terminology of the usuliyyun, the term 'proof' applies to a single term. Imam Ibn al-Humam said in his Tahreer: 'So it is a single term, and it may be the subject of the sought-after, like the world.' (p.34) Rather, according to the usuliyyun, every single term whose state is such that by correct consideration of its states the sought-after news can be reached is a proof. So those who have restricted proof to a single term and yet considered observation and inference in the instance of proof have seemingly overlooked the condition of possibility, because observation and inference are not actually obligatory in the proof; rather, it is sufficient that the proof has the potential for inference. Also, for observation and inference, at least two premises are necessary, so inference in a single term can be considered? It is remembered that under this interpretation, proof covers both definitive and conjectural, because in the definition of proof, 'leading to' means both leading to knowledge and leading to conjecture, whether that leading is productive and preparatory or necessary and customary. By explaining in this way, the definition of proof can be correct for everyone. His statement: 'Sometimes it is restricted to the definitive' — due to consideration of generality in the meaning of proof, the author said that sometimes proof is specified with the definitive, which leads to certainty. In that case, the sought-after news is restricted with the condition of certainty, or they add the word 'knowledge' before the sought-after news and say: 'leading to knowledge of the sought-after news'. For this reason, Sayf al-Amidi said in Ihkam that the definition mentioned is according to the usul jurists, but according to the usul custom, the definition of proof is: 'That by which one can reach knowledge of a sought-after news', which is the inclination of the author of al-Mahsul and the author of al-Minhaj. Restricting proof to knowledge and certainty excludes those proofs that are purely beneficial for conjectures. As for the conjectural proof that leads to the sought-after by way of conjecture or imagination, it is called 'imara' (indication) because it is a sign for the sought-after, it does not give knowledge of the sought-after. His statement: 'Production is based on three [elements]' — It has passed that the logicians do not apply the term 'proof' to a single term; rather, in their view, proof is the name of a compound of two statements from which another statement necessarily follows. This shows that according to the logician, composition is necessary in proof. Contrary to this, according to the usuli, composition does not occur in proof except in the sense that observation and inference are linked to composition. The author indicated this meaning with his statement 'production'. Its conclusion is that a single-term proof cannot produce a result and reach the sought-after while it is a single term; but for a single-term proof to produce a result, the existence of three matters is necessary. And this is because a single-term proof by itself cannot reach the sought-after. Therefore, a subject of the sought-after, a predicate of the sought-after, and a middle term between them are necessary, which connects the two to each other. The author indicated this meaning with 'because a middle term is necessary', and the middle term must be between the two sides of the sought-after, i.e., the subject of judgment and the predicate of judgment; otherwise, the mind will not transfer from the second to the first, because from one of the disparate things, reaching the other is not possible. Therefore, a matter that implies both is necessary, such that when the predicate is established for it, it becomes necessarily established for the subject. Then that connection which is correct for production is found. Thus, for the proof to be productive, two premises are necessary. The first premise is called the minor premise and the second premise the major premise, and the subject and predicate of its result are called the minor and major terms. Since the first [premise] is on the subject of the sought-after and the middle term, and the second...
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Nu‘ut, a commentary on Muslim al-Thubut, explains the concept of 'dalil' (proof) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh and contrasts it with the logicians' (mantiqiyyun) understanding. The author begins by illustrating how one can know that the world has a creator through correct consideration of its change and origination. In this inference, the usuliyyun consider the single term 'world' (al-‘alam) as the dalil, whereas logicians consider both premises ('the world is changing' and 'everything changing is originated') as the dalil. The author emphasizes that in usul terminology, dalil is a mufrad (singular term) that, through proper observation of its states, can lead to the sought-after knowledge. He cites Ibn al-Humam who states that the dalil may be the subject of the sought-after, like 'the world'. The passage then clarifies that some scholars restrict dalil to qat‘i (definitive) proofs that yield certainty, while others include zanni (conjectural) proofs as well. When restricted to qat‘i, the definition includes the word 'knowledge', as preferred by al-Amidi and others. The author then addresses the relationship between the single-term dalil and the requirement of inference: for a dalil to be productive (muntij), it must involve a middle term (wasita) that connects the subject and predicate. This requires at least two premises (muqaddamatan) — the minor and major premises. Thus, although the dalil itself is a single term, the inferential process requires composition. The passage thus concisely defines the Hanafi usuli concept of dalil and distinguishes it from the logical definition.

Key Terms
دلیل (dalīl): A single term (mufrad) that, through correct consideration of its states, can lead to knowledge or conjecture of a sought-after news.
مفرد (mufrad): A single term (like 'the world') that serves as the proof itself.
مطلوب خبری (maṭlūb khabarī): A sought-after proposition that is to be proven, e.g., 'the world is created'.
قیاسی (qiyāsī): Inferential; the deduction process using two premises.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the usuliyyun (Hanafi usul scholars), what constitutes a proof (dalil)?
A) A compound of two premises that necessarily produce a conclusion.
B) A single term that, through correct observation of its states, can lead to the sought-after.
C) Any statement that leads to certainty.
D) The minor and major premises together.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a proof (dalil) is a single term (mufrad) that, through correct observation (nazar sahih), can lead to knowledge or conjecture of a sought-after news; it is not the compound of two premises.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

Nazar as a Source of Knowledge: Refuting the Sumaniyya
Refuting the Sumaniyya's denial of nazar's knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rebuttal to the Sumaniyya's denial of nazar as a source of knowledge and identify the thamarah al-khilaf for usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
Correct nazar produces certain knowledge; sense-perception also errs, so the Sumaniyya's objection fails.

Can rational reflection (nazar) yield certain knowledge? The Sumaniyya, a materialist sect, argued that only sense-perception can produce knowledge, and nazar often leads to error indistinguishable from truth. This passage from Ashraf al-Nu'ut presents the Hanafi response: the same skepticism applies to sense-perception, yet we accept it; moreover, self-evident truths are known without senses. Understanding this dispute is crucial for justifying qiyas and istihsan in Hanafi usul.

Guided Path
1
State the Sumaniyya position
Identify the claim: only sense-perception (hiss) gives knowledge; nazar (rational reflection) does not because it often results in error (jahl) that resembles true knowledge.
Their argument is an epistemological skepticism.
2
Analyze their dalil
The Sumaniyya argue that nazar produces contradictory conclusions and that certainty (jasm) from nazar is indistinguishable from ignorance, so we cannot know if any result is true.
They cite the prevalence of disagreement among rational thinkers.
3
Present the Hanafi rebuttal
First, self-evident truths (badihiyyat) are known without senses. Second, sense-perception also errs (e.g., optical illusions). So the Sumaniyya's objection applies equally to their own criterion. Third, correct nazar that fulfills conditions yields certain knowledge; the possibility of error does not negate actual correct cases.
The Hanafi position is that nazar, when sound, is a valid source.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
If nazar produces knowledge, then qiyas and istihsan are valid sources of law. If not, only textual proofs and sense-based evidence would be accepted. This directly affects the structure of Hanafi usul al-fiqh.
The practical consequence is the legitimacy of rational legal reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اشرف النعوت شرح مسلم الثبوت ۱۶۱ الى المقاطع مثلا بمثل والحس لايفيد الاعلماً جزئياً وهو لا يكون كاسباً بل الحق منع التماثل كما هو مذهبنا فتدبر ترجمه : یعنی سمیہ نے نظر کے افادہ علم سے انکار کیا ہے انکا کہنا ہے کہ علم صرف حس سے حاصل ہوتا ہے کیونکہ جزم کبھی جہل ہوتا ہے اور وہ علم کے مثل ہے تو پھر کس طرح یہ معلوم ہوسکیگا کہ نظر کے بعد جو حاصل ہوا ہے وہ علم ہی ہے اسکا جواب یہ دیا جاتا ہے کہ وہ عوارض سے ممتاز ہوتا ہے کیونکہ نظر صحیح کے بعد بداہت عقل حکم لگا دیتی ہے کہ وہ علم ہی ہے جہل نہیں ہے۔ اس پر میں کہتا ہوں کہ یہ کیسے جانا جائے گا کہ نظر صحیح ہے چونکہ احتمال خطاء کا مبادی سے لیکر مقاطع تک جوں کا توں رہتا ہے اور حس تو وہ صرف علم جزئی کا فائدہ دیتی ہے جو کا سب نہیں ہوتی۔ بلکہ حق یہ ہے کہ تماثل ممنوع ہے جیسا کہ ہمارا مذہب ہے لہذ افکر سے کام لو ! تشریح : ۔ جب ماتن نظر کی تعریف، اس کے حکم اور اس کے اقسام جو موصل الی المطلوب ہیں سے فارغ ہو گئے تو مفاد نظر کے بیان کرنے کا ارادہ کیا ، مفاد نظریہ ہے کہ نظر مطلقا تصورات وتصدیقات کے علم کا فائدہ دیتا ہے، مفاد نظر کا بیان اس لئے ضروری سمجھا کہ اس میں عقلاء کازبردست اختلاف ہے یہاں تک کہ ان میں سے بعض نے جیسے سمنیہ ، ملاحدہ نے مطلقاً نظر کے افادہ علم سے انکار کیا ہے اور بعض جیسے فلاسفہ نے خصوصاً الہیات میں افادہ نظر کے جرم سے انکار کیا پس مصنف نے ان لوگوں کے رد و ابطال کا ارادہ کیا جنہوں نے مطلقا نظر کے افادہ سے انکار کیا ہے اس لئے کہا السمنية نفوا الخ۔ قوله - السمنية سمنہوس کے ضمہ اور میم کے فتح کے ساتھ آیا ہے سومنات کی طرف منسوب ہے بت پرستوں کا ایک طبقہ جو تناسخ کا عقیدہ رکھتا ہے۔ سمنیہ کہلاتا ہے ان کا عقیدہ ہے کہ جو سومنات مندر کی زیارت کرلیے گا اس کی روح تناسخ سے محفوظ رہے گی جیسا کہ شرح طوالع وغیرہ میں ہے اور شامی نے انساب میں ذکر کیا ہے کہ سمنیہ وہ دھریہ ہے جو تناسخ کا عقیدہ رکھتے ہیں ان کی رہائش گاہ ہندوستان ہے سومنات کی طرف منسوب ہے اور سومنات ان کی ایک بڑی تیر تھ گاہ ہے۔ قوله - نفوا افادة النظر العلم قائلين بان لاعلم الابالحس الخ اس عبارت میں سمنیہ کا نظریہ بیان کیا ہے۔ سمنیہ کہتے ہیں کہ نظر معقل سے کوئی علم حاصل نہیں ہوسکتا اگر کسی سے علم حاصل ہو سکتا ہے تو وہ صرف حس ہے ان کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ نظر سے جو جزم حاصل ہوتا ہے وہ کبھی خلاف واقعہ بھی ہوتا ہے اور یہ سراسر جہل ہے اور یہ جہل جو بعد نظر حاصل ہوا ہے بعینہ اس علم واقع مطابق کی طرح ہے جو علم کہ نظر کے بعد حاصل ہوتا ہے پس جب نظر سے علم و جہل دونوں حاصل ہوتے ہیں تو پھر کس طرح یہ معلوم ہو سکے گا کہ نظر سے جو ہم کو حاصل ہوا ہے علم ہی ہے جہل نہیں جسمغنیہ کی دلیل کی مزید توضیح یہ ہے کہ نظر کے بعد جو جزم حاصل ہوتا ہے اس میں اکثر غلطیاں ہو جایا کرتی ہیں کہ نظر سے حاصل شدہ علم کے بارے میں آراء متناقض ہیں چنانچہ بہت سے عقلاء نظر کرتے ہیں تو اس کو اس نظر سے ایسا مطلوب حاصل ہوتا ہے جو ناظر اول کے مطلوب سے مناقض ہوتا ہے اور یہ بدیہی ہے کہ بیک وقت دونوں مطلوب ایک واقعہ کے مطابق نہیں ہو سکتے ورنہ اجتماع نقیضین لازم آئے گا اور یہ محال اور جوستلزم محال ہے وہ محال ہوتا ہے لہذا نظر سے واقع کے مطابق علم کا حاصل ہونا محال ہے یہی مذہب ملاحدہ کا ہے اور باب الہیاب میں فلاسفہ کا بھی یہی نظریہ ہے۔ اس کے برخلاف جمہور عقلاء کا مذہب مختار یہ ہے کہ ہر وہ نظر جو صیح ہو تجمع شرائط ہو اور مواقع سب مرتفع ہوں وہ تصورات، خواہ تصدیقات البیہ وغیرہ سب میں علم کا افادہ کرتا ہے کیونکہ جو شخص نظر صحیح مستجمع شرائط پر نظر رکھے اور ساتھ ہی حال لازم اس کے پیش نظر رہے تو اس کو جزم حاصل ہوتا ہے کہ بر نظر صیح علم بالجزم کا دابتہ افادہ کرتا ہے۔ رہے نا فین کا یہ کہنا کہ حصول علم کا ذریعہ صرف حس سے صحیح نہیں اولا اس لئے کہ ہمکو بدیہات کا علم حاصل ہوتا ہے جن میں حس کو مطلقا دخل نہیں جیسے ہمارا قول کل مضايفة لا يوجد بدون الآخر ثانیا حس بھی بے شمار غلطیاں کرتا ہے چنانچہ حائے بصر، صغیر کو کبیر ، کبیر کوصغیر واحد کو اثنین اور اثنین کو اربعہ دیکھتا ہے، یونہی حاسہ ذائقہ میٹھا کو کڑوا پاتا ہے وغیرہ ۔ غرض بذریعہ حس علم حاصل ہونے کے بعد جو جزم حاصل ہوتا ہے وہ کبھی جہل ، غیر مطابق للواقع ہوتا ہے اس لئے حس بھی اس جزم کا افادہ نہیں کر سکتا جو جس کہ علم مطابق للواقع کا جزم پیدا کرتا ہے۔ حاصل یہ کہ نظر کی طرح جس کے ذریعہ حاصل شدہ جہل بھی علم کے مثل ہو گیا تو پھر کیسے جانا جائے گا کہ۔ جس کے ذریعہ جو حاصل ہوا ہے وہ علم ہی ہے جہل نہیں ۔ فما هو جوابكم فهو جوابنا - شرح عقائد میں علامہ سعدالدین تفتازانی نے سمعیہ و ملاحدہ کے نظریہ کا بطلان کرتے ہوئے کہا ہے کہ بعض نظر کا فساد کے
Ashraf al-Nu'ut Sharh Muslim al-Thubut. 161. ...to the endpoints like for like, and sense does not give knowledge except partially, and it does not acquire. Rather, the truth is the denial of similarity as is our school, so reflect. Translation: That is, the Samaniyya denied that nazar gives knowledge. They say knowledge is only obtained from sense-perception, because certainty is sometimes ignorance and it is similar to knowledge, so how can it be known that what is obtained after nazar is knowledge? The answer is that it is distinguished by attributes, because after correct nazar, the intellect's intuition judges that it is knowledge, not ignorance. To this I say: How will it be known that the nazar is correct? Since the possibility of error remains from the beginning to the end just as before, and sense only gives partial knowledge, which is not comprehensive. Rather, the truth is that similarity is denied, as is our school. So use reflection! Commentary: When the author finished the definition of nazar, its ruling, and its types that lead to the goal, he intended to mention the benefit of nazar. The benefit is that nazar absolutely gives knowledge of concepts and judgments. He deemed it necessary because there is strong disagreement among intellectuals. Some, like the Sumaniyya and the materialists, absolutely denied the benefit of nazar; others, like the philosophers, denied the benefit of nazar specifically in theology. So the author intended to refute those who absolutely denied the benefit of nazar. Hence he said: 'The Sumaniyya denied...' His statement: 'al-Sumaniyya' - it comes with dammah on the sin and fatha on the mim, attributed to Somnath. A sect of idol worshippers who believe in metempsychosis. They are called Sumaniyya. Their belief is that whoever visits the Somnath temple will have his soul saved from metempsychosis. The commentator Shami mentioned in al-Ansab that the Sumaniyya are materialists who believe in metempsychosis; their abode is India, attributed to Somnath, which is a major temple for them. His statement: 'They denied the benefit of nazar for knowledge, saying there is no knowledge except through sense...' In this statement, the theory of the Sumaniyya is described. The Sumaniyya say that nazar in the intellect cannot bring any knowledge; if knowledge can be obtained from anything, it is only through sense. Their proof is that the certainty obtained from nazar sometimes contradicts reality and is outright ignorance. And this ignorance obtained after nazar is exactly like the real knowledge that is obtained after nazar. So when both knowledge and ignorance are obtained from nazar, how can it be known that what we obtained from nazar is knowledge and not ignorance? Further explanation of their proof: The certainty obtained after nazar often contains errors. There are contradictory opinions about the knowledge obtained from nazar. Many intellectuals perform nazar and obtain a result that contradicts the result of the first investigator. It is obvious that two contradictory results cannot both correspond to one reality, otherwise the conjunction of opposites would be necessary, which is impossible. Therefore, obtaining knowledge corresponding to reality from nazar is impossible. This is the school of the materialists, and the philosophers also hold this view in theology. Contrary to this, the choice of the majority of intellectuals is that every nazar that is correct, fulfills its conditions, and obstacles are removed, gives knowledge in concepts and judgments, self-evident or otherwise. Because the person who performs correct nazar satisfying conditions, and keeps the necessary state in view, obtains certainty that correct nazar inevitably gives certain knowledge. As for the deniers' claim that sense is the only means of obtaining knowledge, it is incorrect: first, because we have knowledge of self-evident truths in which sense has no role, like our statement 'every correlative cannot exist without the other'; second, sense also makes countless errors. The eye sees a small thing as big, a big thing as small, one as two, two as four; similarly, the taste finds sweet as bitter, etc. In short, after obtaining knowledge through sense, the certainty obtained is sometimes ignorance not corresponding to reality. So sense also cannot give the certainty that knowledge corresponding to reality gives. The conclusion: Just as with nazar, the ignorance obtained through it becomes similar to knowledge, then how can it be known? What is obtained through it is knowledge, not ignorance. 'So whatever your answer is, it is our answer.' In Sharh al-Aqa'id, Allama Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani, while refuting the theory of the Sumaniyya and materialists, said that some nazar is corrupt...
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute about the epistemic value of nazar (rational reflection). The author, commenting on the matn of Muslim al-Thubut, refutes the Sumaniyya (a materialist sect from India) who deny that nazar produces any knowledge, claiming that only sense-perception (hiss) yields knowledge. Their argument is that nazar often results in error (jahl) that is indistinguishable from genuine knowledge, making it impossible to know whether any belief formed through nazar is true. The author counters by pointing out that the same objection applies to sense-perception, which also frequently errs (e.g., optical illusions). Moreover, self-evident truths (badihiyyat) are known without any sensory input. The relied-upon position of the majority of intellectuals (including Hanafis) is that correct nazar that fulfills all conditions and is free from obstacles does produce certain knowledge in both concepts (tasawwurat) and judgments (tasdiqat). The passage demonstrates the Hanafi commitment to rational inference as a valid source of knowledge in usul al-fiqh and theology.

Key Terms
نظر (nazar): Rational reflection or critical thinking that leads to knowledge of concepts and judgments.
حس (hiss): Sense-perception through the five senses, which the Sumaniyya claim is the sole source of knowledge.
جزم (jasm): Certainty or firm belief that results from nazar or sense-perception, which may be true (knowledge) or false (ignorance).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main flaw in the Sumaniyya's argument that only sense-perception gives knowledge?
A) Sense-perception never errs, so it is a reliable criterion.
B) Sense-perception also errs, so the same objection applies to their own claim.
C) Nazar always produces certain knowledge without any possibility of error.
D) The Sumaniyya are not Muslim, so their argument is invalid.

Key Takeaway
Correct nazar, when fulfilling conditions, produces certain knowledge; the Sumaniyya's skepticism is refuted by the fallibility of sense-perception and the existence of self-evident truths.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
beginner
6 min
The Ruler and the Senses of Good and Evil
Definition of hakim and clarification of husn and qubh senses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on intellectual good and evil (husn wa qubh) and distinguish the three senses, recognizing that legal reward and punishment depend solely on revelation.

Core Idea
Actions are rationally good or evil, but legal reward and punishment come only from Allah.

This lesson opens the second major section of Musallam al-Thubut on rulings (ahkam). It first establishes the fundamental principle that no ruling has binding force except from Allah. It then clarifies the concept of husn and qubh (good and evil) by distinguishing three distinct senses: as attributes of perfection/deficiency, as compatibility with worldly interests, and as desert of divine reward or punishment. The first two are intellectual and undisputed; the third is the legal sense that is established solely by revelation. Understanding this distinction is critical for correctly deriving rulings in Hanafi usul.

Guided Path
1
State the thesis
Identify the central statement: 'La hukma illa min Allah' – there is no ruling except from Allah. This means that any binding legal evaluation comes only from divine revelation, not from human reason or custom.
This is the foundation of Hanafi usul: the Lawgiver alone has authority to impose obligations.
2
Identify the three meanings of husn and qubh
Locate the three senses listed in the passage: (1) attribute of perfection vs. deficiency, (2) compatibility vs. opposition to worldly purpose, (3) deserving of divine praise/reward vs. blame/punishment.
The first two are intellectual (aqli) and universally agreed upon; the third is the subject of shari'ah.
3
Distinguish the legal sense
Recognize that only the third sense – deserving divine reward or punishment – constitutes the legal ruling (hukm shari). This sense is not determined by reason alone but by revelation.
The Hanafi position affirms rational appraisal but insists that legal consequences depend on the Lawgiver.
4
Apply the distinction
For any action, first assess its rational character (good/bad in the first two senses), then look to revelation for the legal ruling (fard, haram, etc.).
Example: truthfulness is rationally good and beneficial, but its legal obligation is confirmed by revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
متن المسلم : " المقالة الثانية فى الاحكام وفيها ابواب الأول في الحاكم لا حكم الامن الله تعالى لا نزاع فى أن الفعل حسن وقبيح عقلا بمعنى صفة الكمال والنقصان او بمعنى ملايمة الغرض الدنياوى ومنافرته بل معنی استحقاق مدحه تعالى وثوابه و مقابلهما
The text of Musallam: "The second article on rulings, and in it are chapters. The first chapter on the ruler (hakim): There is no ruling except from Allah. There is no dispute that an action is good or evil intellectually, in the sense of the attribute of perfection and deficiency, or in the sense of matching or opposing worldly purpose, but rather in the sense of deserving praise and reward from Him and the opposite of them."
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This is the opening of the second article on ahkam (rulings) from the matn of Musallam al-Thubut. It establishes the fundamental principle that legislation belongs solely to Allah (la hukma illa min Allah). It then clarifies the concept of husn and qubh (good and evil) in three senses: (1) as attributes of perfection or deficiency (e.g., knowledge is good, ignorance is evil); (2) as compatibility or incompatibility with worldly interests; and (3) as deserving divine praise and reward or its opposite. The first two senses are intellectual and not subject to dispute. The third sense is what constitutes legal ruling (hukm shari), and that is solely from Allah. This sets the stage for the discussion of how rulings are known.

Key Terms
حكم (hukm): Legal ruling; specifically, the divine address concerning the acts of the obligated persons.
حسن و قبيح (husn wa qubh): Good and evil; intellectual qualities of actions in terms of perfection/deficiency, worldly benefit/harm, and desert of divine reward/punishment.
حاكم (hakim): Ruler or lawgiver; in usul, the one who issues rulings (hukm). The passage declares that the only true hakim is Allah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the matn, in which sense is there no dispute that actions are good or evil?
A) In the sense of deserving divine praise and reward.
B) In the sense of being perfect or deficient, and matching or opposing worldly purpose.
C) In the sense of being commanded or prohibited by the Lawgiver.
D) In the sense of being beneficial or harmful to others.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position distinguishes rational good/evil (intellectual) from legal good/evil (revelational), affirming the former but grounding binding rulings solely in divine revelation.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
8 min
Allah is the Sole Source of Shar'i Rulings
Introducing the second article on rulings and affirming Allah as the sole Ruler.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of hukm and explain why only Allah is the absolute Ruler (Hakim), citing the Quranic proof and consensus.

Core Idea
Rulings (ahkam) are only from Allah; hukm is His address to the responsible person.

In the study of usul al-fiqh, we first established the four proofs as the sources of rulings. Now we turn to the rulings themselves: what are they, and where do they come from? This lesson introduces the fundamental principle that all rulings originate exclusively from Allah. Understanding this sets the foundation for all subsequent discussions on how rulings are derived from revelation and why human reason, while valuable, is not an independent source of legislative authority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject of the second article
The passage announces the second article is about rulings (ahkam). Recall that usul al-fiqh studies the proofs as sources; rulings are what those proofs establish.
The article has four chapters: Hakim, Hukm, Mahkum fihi (actions), Mahkum alayh (mukallaf).
2
Define 'hukm' according to the Hanafi school
The author defines hukm as 'the address of Allah related to the actions of the responsible persons.' This is the core taklifi definition. It covers commands, prohibitions, and permissions.
Note the plural 'ahkam' is used to include rulings from various sciences (usul, fiqh, kalam).
3
Analyze the Quranic evidence and consensus
The phrase 'لا حكم إلا من الله' is derived from Quran 12:40. The passage states that the Ummah is unanimous that only Allah is the absolute Ruler. Identify the proof's structure: a definitive text (Quran) and a definitive consensus (ijma).
The particle 'illa' indicates exclusive limitation.
4
Examine the Mu'tazila dispute
Some earlier Hanafi scholars claimed Mu'tazila consider reason as a source of ruling. The passage clarifies that Mu'tazila actually hold reason only 'knows' some self-evident rulings, not that it legislates. Understand the nuance: the dispute is about the scope of reason's role.
The text resolves that the Hanafi position—that only Allah is the Ruler—is the truth supported by consensus.
5
Consider the multiple interpretations of 'hukm'
The passage explores three possible meanings for 'hukm': (1) the address itself, (2) the effect of the address (obligation/prohibition), or (3) the action or omission considered upon the servant. The chosen meaning for this context is the address itself, to maintain the exclusivity of Allah as the only source of address.
This step shows how careful definition prevents misinterpretation.

Source Text

اشرف النعوت شرح مسلم الثبوت
۱۷۲
ترجمه : ۔ مقالہ ثانیہ احکام کے بیان میں ہے اور اس میں چند ابواب میں باب اول حاکم کے ذکر میں ہے حکم صرف اللہ ہی کے لئے ہے عقلی طور پر فعل کے حسن و قبیح ہونے میں صفت کمال و نقصان یا غرض دنیاوی کے موافقت و مخالفت کے معنی کے اعتبار سے کوئی نزاع نہیں ہے بلکہ حسن و فتح کے معنی اللہ تعالیٰ کے مدح و ثواب اور ان کے بالمقابل ذم و عقاب کے استحقاق کے معنی میں اختلاف ہے۔ تشريح - قوله المقالة الثانية فى الاحكام
مقالات تثلیثہ میں سے مقالہ اولی سے فراغت حاصل کر لینے کے بعد ماتن نے مقالہ ثانیہ کو بیان کیا۔ غالباً تمکو یاد ہوگا کہ اصول فقہ کا موضوع مصنف کے نزدیک ادلہ اربعہ ہیں اس حیثیت سے کہ وہ احکام کے لئے مثبت ہیں پس احکام متعلقات موضوع سے ہیں اس لئے کہا " المقالة الثانية في الاحکام اور مقالہ ثانیہ چار ابواب پر مشتمل ہے اسلئے کہ اس مقالہ میں یا تو نفس حکم ذکر کیا جائے گا یا حکم کے ضروری امور ثلاثہ یعنی حاکم محکوم علیہ، اورمحکوم فیہ کا بیان ہوگا۔
ماتن کا قول احکام حکم کی جمع ہے ۔ حکم خطاب اللہ تعالیٰ المتعلق بافعال المتکلفین سے عبارت ہے جیسا کہ عنقریب انشاء اللہ آئے گا اور وہ مسائل فقہیہ کے محمولات سے ہے جیسے وجوب، ندب، حرمت، کراہت واباحت ، چنانچہ یہ سب مسائل فقہیہ میں سے کسی نہ کسی مسئلہ کا محمول واقع ہوتے ہیں کہا جاتا ہے الصلوۃ واجبة الخمر حرام وغیرہ احکام بعض اصول سے بعض فقہ سے اور بعض علم کلام وغیرہ سے متعلق ہیں پس اسی لئے ماتن نے چند علوم کے اعتبار سے اس کے مختلف اقسام کی طرف نظر کرتے ہوئے حکم مفرد کے بجائے جمع الا حکام استعمال کیا چنانچہ یہی وجہ ہے کہ احکام کو مطلق رکھا فقہیہ کی قید سے مقید کر کے الاحکام الفقہیہ نہ کہا کہ جمیع احکام کا فقہ سے ہونا پردہ خفا میں ہے رہے بعض احکام کا فقہ سے ہونا تو یہ استحقاق نہیں رکھتا ہے کہ احکام کو فقہیہ سے مقید کیا جائے ۔
قوله وفيها ابواب
مشتمل ہے
فيها کی ضمیر مقالہ ثانیہ کی طرف راجع ہے معنی یہ ہے کہ مقالہ ثانیہ چار ابواب پرست باب اول حاکم کے بیان میں ہے اور حاکم وہ ہے جس کا حکم صادر ہو، باب دوم حکم کے بیان میں ہے باب سوم محکوم فیہ کے بیان میں ہے اور وہ فعل ہے اور باب چہارم محکوم علیہ کے بیان میں ہے اور وہ مکلف کو کہتے ہیں۔
قوله لا حكم الامن الله تعالى
درج بالا عبارت کا مفہوم ظاہر ہے اور وہ یہ ہے اس عالم رنگ و بو میں حکم صرف اور صرف اللہ عز شانہ تعالیٰ کا حکم ہے چنانچہ امت مسلمہ کا اجماع اس پر ہے کہ حکم حقیقتہ صرف اللہ ہی کا ہے جیسا کہ اسنوی نے شرح منہاج میں ، ابن ہمام نے تحریر میں اس کی تصریح فرمائی اور ابن امیر الحاج نے تقریر میں اقرار کیا ہے کہ اللہ تعالیٰ کا حاکم مطلق ہونا اجماعی مسئلہ ہے۔ ایسا نہیں کہ اہلسنت و جماعت ہی اللہ کو علی الاطلاق حاکم مانتے ہیں اور معتزلہ اللہ کے علاوہ عقل کو بھی حاکم جانتے ہیں جیسا کہ مشائخ اہلسنت. میں صاحب اصول بزدوی، صاحب توضیح اور صاحب شرح مختصر وغیر ہم کا خیال انکا کہنا ہے کہ معتزلہ کے نزدیک عقل بھی حاکم ہے۔ مگر حق بات یہ ہے کہ معتزلہ عقل کے حاکم ہونے کا قول نہیں کرتے بلکہ وہ یہ کہتے ہیں کہ عقل بعض احکام البیہ کا معرف ہے خواہ درود شرع ہو یا نہ ہو، لیکن معتزلہ پر یہ الزام بہر حال قائم ہے کہ انہوں نے خلقت افعال باری تعالی میں جب شرک پر جرات کر لی بایں طور کہ انہوں نے بندہ کو بلکہ بندہ کے افعال کو افعال بندہ کا خالق گردانا تو ان سے کوئی بعید نہیں کہ حکم باری تعالیٰ میں شریک ٹھہرالیں اس حیثیت سے صاحب اصول بزدوی صاحب توضیح و غیرہ کا قول زیادہ قرین قیاس معلوم ہوتا ہے۔
ماتن کی عبارت " لا حكم الا من الله تعالى الله عز شانہ کے ارشادان الحكم الا للہ سے مستفاد ہے اور اس عبارت کے معنی کا صحیح محمل جاننے کے لئے مراد حکم جاننا ضروری ہے پس یادر ہے کہ اہل شرع کے نزدیک حکم سے مراد یا تو حکم تکلیف ہے اور حکم تکلیف یا تو خطاب اللہ تعالیٰ المتعلق با فعال المكلفين اقتضاء و تخییر اسے عبارت ہے یا اس سے اثر خطاب وجوب ، حرمت مراد ہے یا پھر اس سے وہ فعل یا کف فعل مراد ہے جو شارع نے ذمہ عبد میں اعتبار کیا ہے پس اس تقدیر پر ضروری ہے کہ معنی حکم تجرید کر لیا جائے تا کہ حکم سے مطلق خطاب مراد لیا جانا شیخ ہو سکے اب ان وجوہ کی بناء پر مصنف کی عبارت کا مطلب یہ ہوگا " لا خطاب الامن اللہ تعالیٰ یہ بھی ممکن ہے کہ حکم سے حکم ایجا دو تکوین مراد ہو جو خلق کے معنی آتا ہے اس تقدیر پر اہل حق کے نزدیک معنی یہ ہوگا کہ خلق و ایجاد نہیں
The second article is about rulings and in it are several chapters: the first chapter mentions the Ruler. Rulings are only for Allah. Intellectually, regarding an action's beauty (husn) and ugliness (qubh), there is no dispute in the sense of perfection/deficiency or worldly benefit/harm. Rather, the dispute is in the meaning of beauty and ugliness as deserving praise and reward from Allah, and opposite blame and punishment. Commentary: His statement: 'The second article on rulings' – After finishing the first article, the author (matn) presents the second article. You likely recall that the subject of usul al-fiqh according to the author is the four proofs, in that they establish rulings. Thus rulings are from the subject's related matters. So he said 'the second article on rulings'. And the second article comprises four chapters, because in this article either the ruling itself is mentioned or the three necessary aspects of a ruling: the Ruler, the ruled upon (person), and the thing ruled upon. The author's statement: 'rulings (ahkam)' is the plural of 'hukm'. Hukm is the address of Allah Most High related to the actions of the responsible persons, as will come soon inshallah. And it is the predicate of fiqhi issues, such as obligation (wujub), recommendation (nadb), prohibition (hurma), dislike (karaha), and permission (ibaha). Thus all these are predicates of some fiqhi issue: it is said 'prayer is obligatory', 'wine is prohibited', etc. Rulings relate partly to usul, partly to fiqh, partly to kalam and other sciences. Therefore the author used the plural 'ahkam' instead of the singular 'hukm', considering its different types from various sciences. This is why he kept 'ahkam' absolute and did not restrict it by 'fiqhiyya', because not all rulings are from fiqh; some are from other sciences. His statement: 'and in it are chapters' – the pronoun returns to the second article. Meaning: the second article consists of four chapters: first chapter on the Ruler – and the Ruler is the one from whom the ruling issues; second chapter on the ruling; third chapter on the thing ruled upon, which is the action; fourth chapter on the ruled upon (person), who is the responsible person (mukallaf). His statement: 'There is no ruling except from Allah' – the meaning of the above phrase is clear: in this world of color and scent, ruling is only from Allah, Majestic is His glory. The consensus of the Muslim community is that in reality, ruling belongs only to Allah, as al-Isnawi clarified in 'Sharh al-Minhaj', Ibn al-Humam in 'al-Tahrir', and Ibn Amir al-Haj affirmed in 'al-Taqrir' that Allah's being the absolute Ruler is a matter of consensus. It is not that only Ahl al-Sunna wal-Jama'a believe Allah is the absolute Ruler while the Mu'tazila consider reason also as a ruler besides Allah, as some scholars of Ahl al-Sunna such as the author of 'Usul al-Bazdawi', the author of 'al-Tawdih', the author of 'Sharh al-Mukhtasar' etc. thought they say. However, the truth is that the Mu'tazila do not say that reason is a ruler; rather they say that reason is a knower of some self-evident rulings, whether or not the Shari'ah supports them. But this accusation still stands against them: when they dared to associate partners in the creation of actions of Allah Most High, considering the servant and his actions as creators of the servant's actions, then it is not far from them to also make a partner in Allah's ruling. In this respect, the statement of the author of 'Usul al-Bazdawi', 'al-Tawdih', and others appears more plausible. The author's phrase 'There is no ruling except from Allah' is derived from Allah's statement 'Inna al-hukma illa lillah' (Quran). To understand the correct interpretation of this phrase, it is necessary to know the intended meaning of 'hukm'. So remember that according to the people of Shari'ah, 'hukm' either means: (1) the ruling of obligation (hukm taklifi), which is the address of Allah Most High related to the actions of the responsible persons implying a demand or option; or (2) the effect of the address – obligation, prohibition; or (3) the action or omission that the Lawgiver considers upon the servant's responsibility. On this assumption, it is necessary to abstract the meaning of 'hukm' so that it can be possible to interpret 'hukm' as absolute address. Now based on these aspects, the meaning of the author's phrase would be: 'There is no address except from Allah'. It is also possible that 'hukm' means the creative decree (hukm takwini) which means creation. On this assumption, according to the people of truth, the meaning would be: 'There is no creation and bringing into existence'
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage introduces the second article of the text, which deals with rulings (ahkam). The author explains that 'hukm' is defined as the address of Allah related to the actions of responsible persons (mukallafin). This address encompasses five types: obligation, recommendation, prohibition, dislike, and permission. The text emphasizes that rulings in reality belong only to Allah, citing the Quranic verse 'Inna al-hukma illa lillah' and consensus of the Muslim community. A brief discussion addresses the Mu'tazila position on reason; the author refutes the claim that Mu'tazila consider reason as a source of ruling, though he notes that some earlier Hanafi scholars attribute that view to them. Ultimately, the text asserts that the absolute Ruler (al-Hakim) is Allah alone. The passage also outlines that the second article has four chapters: (1) the Ruler (al-Hakim), (2) the Ruling (al-Hukm), (3) the Thing Ruled Upon (al-Mahkum fihi) – actions, and (4) the Person Ruled Upon (al-Mahkum alayh) – the mukallaf. The author clarifies why the plural 'ahkam' is used instead of 'hukm': to include rulings from various sciences (usul, fiqh, kalam) since not all rulings are purely fiqhi. The commentary then explores multiple interpretations of 'hukm': as the address itself, as the effect of the address (obligation/prohibition), or as the action/omission considered by the Lawgiver. It concludes that the intended meaning is the absolute address of Allah, from which no ruling originates except from Him.

Key Terms
حكم (hukm): The address of Allah related to the actions of the responsible persons (taklifi).
حاكم (hakim): The one from whom the ruling issues; in absolute sense, only Allah.
مكلف (mukallaf): The person addressed by the Shar'i rulings, responsible for actions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary evidence for the statement 'there is no ruling except from Allah'?
A) A hadith from the Prophet
B) The Quranic verse 'Inna al-hukma illa lillah' and the consensus of the Ummah
C) Rational argument alone
D) The opinion of Imam Abu Hanifa

Key Takeaway
Rulings (ahkam) are exclusively the address of Allah to the responsible person; no human reason or authority can create a Shari'i ruling.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
12 min
Allah as the Sole Lawgiver: Analysis of 'La Hukma Illa Min Allah'
Explaining exclusive divine legislation and refuting Mu'tazila

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that Allah alone is the absolute lawgiver and explain why the Mu'tazila are not considered to make reason a co-legislator, while also distinguishing the four meanings of 'hukm'.

Core Idea
Judgment (hukm) in all forms—legislative, creative, decree, and eschatological—belongs exclusively to Allah alone.

This lesson unpacks the foundational usul principle 'There is no judgment except from Allah.' The commentator affirms this as a matter of ijma (consensus), then addresses a common accusation against the Mu'tazila. Finally, he provides a nuanced analysis of the term 'hukm' across four distinct meanings, each reinforcing divine exclusivity.

Guided Path
1
Affirm the Principle
Identify the core statement: 'La hukma illa min Allah'. Understand that the Hanafi school holds this as an absolute, supported by ijma.
This is the base matn.
2
Examine the Ijma Proof
The commentator cites scholars like al-Asnawi, Ibn al-Humam, and Ibn Amir al-Haj to show that Allah being the absolute Judge is a consensus of the ummah.
Ijma is a proof of the principle.
3
Analyze the Mu'tazila Dispute
Distinguish between the accusation and the actual Mu'tazila position. They claim reason is a cognizer (mu'arif) of clear rulings, not a lawgiver. Understand the Hanafi response: their shirk in creation logically leads to partnership in legislation.
The dispute is over the role of reason.
4
Distinguish the Four Meanings of Hukm
Work through each meaning: hukm taklifi (legislative address), hukm takwini (creative act), hukm qada wa qadr (decree), and hukm akhira (final judgment in the Hereafter). For each, confirm that 'la hukma illa min Allah' holds.
This terminological analysis is a hallmark of usul al-fiqh.

قوله لا حكم الامن من الله تعالى
درج بالا عبارت کا مفہوم ظاہر ہے اور وہ یہ ہے اس عالم رنگ و بو میں حکم صرف اور صرف اللہ عز شانہ تعالیٰ کا حکم ہے چنانچہ امت مسلمہ کا اجماع اس پر ہے کہ حکم حقیقتہ صرف اللہ ہی کا ہے جیسا کہ اسنوی نے شرح منہاج میں ، ابن ہمام نے تحریر میں اس کی تصریح فرمائی اور ابن امیر الحاج نے تقریر میں اقرار کیا ہے کہ اللہ تعالیٰ کا حاکم مطلق ہونا اجماعی مسئلہ ہے۔ ایسا نہیں کہ اہلسنت و جماعت ہی اللہ کو علی الاطلاق حاکم مانتے ہیں اور معتزلہ اللہ کے علاوہ عقل کو بھی حاکم جانتے ہیں جیسا کہ مشائخ اہلسنت. میں صاحب اصول بزدوی، صاحب توضیح اور صاحب شرح مختصر وغیر ہم کا خیال انکا کہنا ہے کہ معتزلہ کے نزدیک عقل بھی حاکم ہے۔ مگر حق بات یہ ہے کہ معتزلہ عقل کے حاکم ہونے کا قول نہیں کرتے بلکہ وہ یہ کہتے ہیں کہ عقل بعض احکام البیہ کا معرف ہے خواہ درود شرع ہو یا نہ ہو، لیکن معتزلہ پر یہ الزام بہر حال قائم ہے کہ انہوں نے خلقت افعال باری تعالی میں جب شرک پر جرات کر لی بایں طور کہ انہوں نے بندہ کو بلکہ بندہ کے افعال کو افعال بندہ کا خالق گردانا تو ان سے کوئی بعید نہیں کہ حکم باری تعالیٰ میں شریک ٹھہرالیں اس حیثیت سے صاحب اصول بزدوی صاحب توضیح و غیرہ کا قول زیادہ قرین قیاس معلوم ہوتا ہے۔
ماتن کی عبارت " لا حكم الا من الله تعالى الله عز شانہ کے ارشادان الحكم الا للہ سے مستفاد ہے اور اس عبارت کے معنی کا صحیح محمل جاننے کے لئے مراد حکم جاننا ضروری ہے پس یادر ہے کہ اہل شرع کے نزدیک حکم سے مراد یا تو حکم تکلیف ہے اور حکم تکلیف یا تو خطاب اللہ تعالیٰ المتعلق با فعال المكلفين اقتضاء و تخییر اسے عبارت ہے یا اس سے اثر خطاب وجوب ، حرمت مراد ہے یا پھر اس سے وہ فعل یا کف فعل مراد ہے جو شارع نے ذمہ عبد میں اعتبار کیا ہے پس اس تقدیر پر ضروری ہے کہ معنی حکم تجرید کر لیا جائے تا کہ حکم سے مطلق خطاب مراد لیا جانا شیخ ہو سکے اب ان وجوہ کی بناء پر مصنف کی عبارت کا مطلب یہ ہوگا " لا خطاب الامن اللہ تعالیٰ یہ بھی ممکن ہے کہ حکم سے حکم ایجا دو تکوین مراد ہو جو خلق کے معنی آتا ہے اس تقدیر پر اہل حق کے نزدیک معنی یہ ہوگا کہ خلق و ایجاد نہیں مگر اللہ سے ، اس معنی کی بنا پر معتزلہ کا رد ہو گا جنہوں نے بندہ کو افعال کا خالق مانکر اہل حق کی مخالفت کی ہے۔ یہ بھی ہو سکتا ہے کہ حکم سے حکم قضاء وقد ر مراد ہو، اس تقدیر پر معنی یہ ہوگا کہ قضاء وقد نہیں ہے مگر اللہ کا، چنانچہ الہ عز شانہ کے علم قدیم میں ہر حکم مقدر ہو چکا ہے اور ہرشی کا فیصلہ ہو چکا ہے۔ لہذا اب کسی کا حکم نہیں اس تقدیر پر جنسی حکم کی نفی غیر اللہ سے صحیح ہوگی گو کہ بعض کفار و ظالمین کا حکم بظاہر حکم شرع کے مخالف معلوم ہوتا ہے تاہم وہ بھی حکم قضاء و قدر کے موافق ہے البتہ معتزلہ بندے کے افعال اختیار یہ میں قضاء وقدر سے انکار کرتے ہیں اس لئے کہ ان کے نزدیک بندہ افعال کا حاکم ہے۔ یہ بھی احتمال ہے کہ حکم سے حکم تکلیف نہ مراد ہو بلکہ حکم آخرت مراد ہو، اس صورت میں یہ حکم با نفاق کل عز شانہ کے ساتھ مختص ہے جیسا کہ بادشاہت مختص ہوگی ارشاد ہے لمن الملك اليوم لله الواحد القهار، یاد رہے کہ حکم کے اس معنی کا اختصاص جناب باری تعالیٰ اسمہ کے ساتھ ہونے میں امت اجابت میں سے کسی کا اختلاف نہیں ہے۔
His saying: There is no judgment except from Allah, may He be exalted. The meaning of the above sentence is apparent: in this world of color and smell, judgment is only and solely from Allah, Almighty. Indeed, the consensus of the Muslim nation is that judgment in reality belongs only to Allah, as al-Asnawi clarified in Sharh al-Minhaj, Ibn al-Humam in al-Tahrir, and Ibn Amir al-Haj affirmed in al-Taqrir that Allah being the absolute Judge is a matter of consensus. It is not that only Ahl al-Sunnah believe Allah to be the absolute Judge and the Mu'tazila believe in reason as a judge alongside Allah, as some shaykhs of Ahl al-Sunnah—the author of Usul al-Bazdawi, the author of al-Tawdih, and the author of Sharh al-Mukhtasar—think. They say that according to the Mu'tazila, reason is also a judge. But the correct view is that the Mu'tazila do not claim that reason is a judge; rather they say that reason is a cognizer of some clear rulings, whether or not the Law has come. Nevertheless, the accusation against the Mu'tazila remains valid: since they dared to commit shirk in the creation of Allah's acts by considering the servant, and indeed the servant's acts, as the creator of the servant's actions, it is not unlikely that they would also make a partner in Allah's judgment. From this perspective, the statement of the author of Usul al-Bazdawi, al-Tawdih, and others seems more in line with analogy. The author's phrase 'There is no judgment except from Allah' is derived from Allah's saying 'The judgment is only for Allah'. To know the correct interpretation of its meaning, it is necessary to know the intended meaning of 'judgment'. So remember that according to the people of the Law, judgment (hukm) may refer to a ruling of obligation (hukm taklifi). The ruling of obligation is either the address of Allah related to the actions of the legally responsible persons in terms of demand or option, or it refers to the effect of the address such as obligation or prohibition, or it refers to the act or omission that the Lawgiver has considered in the servant's responsibility. On this basis, the meaning of 'judgment' must be abstracted so that by 'judgment' the absolute address may be intended. Now based on these aspects, the meaning of the author's phrase would be 'There is no address except from Allah'. It is also possible that by 'judgment' is meant the creative and existential judgment, which comes in the meaning of creation. On this basis, according to the people of truth, the meaning is that there is no creation and bringing into existence except from Allah. By this meaning, the Mu'tazila are refuted, who opposed the people of truth by considering the servant the creator of actions. It may also be that by 'judgment' is meant the judgment of destiny and decree (qada wa qadr). On this basis, the meaning is that there is no decree and destiny except Allah's. As such, in Allah's eternal knowledge, every judgment has been destined and every matter has been decided. Therefore, no one's judgment prevails. On this basis, the negation of any judgment from other than Allah is correct, even if some judgments of disbelievers and oppressors apparently contradict the judgment of the Law, yet they are in accordance with the judgment of destiny and decree. However, the Mu'tazila deny destiny and decree in the voluntary actions of the servant because in their view the servant is the judge of actions. There is also a possibility that by 'judgment' is not meant the ruling of obligation but the judgment of the Hereafter. In that case, this judgment is exclusively with Allah, just as sovereignty will be exclusively His, as per the verse: 'To whom belongs the kingdom today? To Allah, the One, the Subduer.' Remember that regarding the exclusivity of this meaning of judgment with Allah, there is no disagreement among any of the people of acceptance.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a ruling and definition: the absolute judgment belongs only to Allah. The commentator begins by stating that the meaning of the matn's phrase 'La hukma illa min Allah' is clear: all legislation comes from Allah alone. He then provides an ijma proof: the Muslim community agrees on this. He cites scholars like al-Asnawi, Ibn al-Humam, and Ibn Amir al-Haj to confirm that this is consensus. Next, he addresses an accusation against the Mu'tazila—that they consider reason as a judge. He clarifies that the Mu'tazila do not actually claim reason is a judge; rather they say reason is a cognizer (mu'arif) of certain clear rulings. However, he argues that their stance on creation (considering the servant a creator of his actions) logically leads to them making partners in judgment, so the earlier scholars' charge is plausible. Then the commentator explains the different possible meanings of 'hukm' in the phrase: 1) hukm taklifi (address of obligation), 2) hukm takwini (creative act), 3) hukm qada wa qadr (decree and destiny), and 4) hukm akhira (judgment of the Hereafter). For each, he shows that the negation 'la hukma illa min Allah' holds. He also refutes the Mu'tazila concerning destiny and free will. The passage concludes that there is complete agreement (ijma) that the final judgment in the afterlife is exclusively Allah's.

Key Terms
حكم (hukm): Divine legislation; an address from Allah concerning the actions of the legally responsible person.
حاكم (hakim): The absolute legislator and judge; Allah alone.
معرف (mu'arif): Cognizer; one who recognizes or knows, without having legislative authority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi consensus mentioned in this passage, who is the absolute lawgiver?
A) Allah alone
B) Allah and the Prophet
C) Allah and human reason
D) The Muslim community through ijma

Key Takeaway
Allah alone is the absolute lawgiver in all senses of hukm—legislative, creative, decree, and eschatological—and this is proven by ijma.

The Three Meanings of Husn and Qubh and the Point of Dispute
Defining the three meanings of good and bad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three meanings of husn and qubh and identify which one is the subject of dispute between the Hanafi/Ash'ari and Mu'tazila schools.

Core Idea
The dispute over rational good and evil concerns only worldly praise and blame.

All Muslims agree that Allah commands good and prohibits evil. The real question is whether we can know good and evil by reason alone, or only through revelation. This passage from a Hanafi usul commentary isolates the precise point of contention: there are three distinct meanings of 'good' and 'bad,' and only one of them is disputed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three meanings of husn and qubh
From the passage context and key terms, recognize the three meanings: (1) perfection/kamal, (2) conformity to purpose, and (3) being a source of praise/blame in this world.
The source text only explicitly mentions the first meaning before cutting off, but the three are well-known in usul.
2
Determine which meanings are agreed upon
All usuliyun (including Mu'tazila and Ash'aris) agree that actions commanded by Allah are good in the sense of perfection and conformity to His purpose. There is no dispute on these two meanings.
3
Isolate the disputed meaning
The third meaning—whether an action is praiseworthy or blameworthy in this world—is the point of disagreement. Mu'tazila argue this can be known by reason; Hanafis and Ash'aris argue it is known only through revelation.
4
Connect to the broader usul debate
This dispute affects how a mujtahid understands moral value: the Hanafi position that moral value derives from revelation influences the methodology of qiyas and istihsan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله لانزاع فى أن الفعل حسن وقبيح عقلا
جب یہ بات معلوم ہوگئی کہ پوری امت مسلمہ کے نزدیک حاکم مطلق صرف اور صرف اللہ تعالیٰ ہے تو اس سے یہ بھی معلوم ہو گیا کہ آمروناهی در حقیقت صرف اللہ ہی ہے پھر یہ ظاہر ہے کہ آمر ونا ہی جس مامور بہ کا حکم دے گا وہ حسن ہوگا اور جس منہی عنہ سے منع کرے گا وہ تیج ہوگا کیونکہ آمرونا ہی اللہ تعالیٰ ہے اور وہ حاکم مطلق ہے اور حکیم نہیں حکم دیتا ہے مگر اسی بات کا جو حسن ہو اور نہیں منع کرتا ہے مگر اس بات سے جو تیج ہو اس لئے اصولین کا اس امر پہ اتفاق ہے کہ مامور بہ کا حسن ہونا اور منھی عنہ کا فتیح ہونا ضروری ہے مگر اختلاف اس میں ہے کہ مامور بہ کا حسن اور منہی عنہ کا فتح کیا لذاتہ ہے جسکو بذریعہ عقل اور اک کیا جا سکتا ہے یا لذاتہ نہیں ہے بلکہ شرعی ہے جس کا حسن و فتح بہت شریعت سے معلوم ہو سکتا ہے اور حسن و فتح کے معنی مشہورہ تین ہیں جن میں محل نزاع و اختلاف صرف ایک معنی ہے اس لئے مصنف نے ان تینوں معانی کو بیان کر کے محل نزاع متعین کرتے ہوئے ہر فریق کے اقوال و دلائل کو ذکر کرنے کا قصد کیا تو کہا لا نزاع في أن الخ۔
ماتن کی ذکر کردہ عبارت کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ حسن و فتح کے تین معانی ہیں (۱) حسن صفت کمال
His saying: 'There is no dispute that the action is good and bad by reason.' When it became known that the absolute judge is only Allah according to the entire Muslim nation, it also became known that the commander and prohibitor is in reality only Allah. Then it is clear that the command of the commander and prohibitor regarding the commanded action will be good, and what He prohibits will be bad, because the commander and prohibitor is Allah, and He is the absolute judge, and the Wise does not command except that which is good, and does not prohibit except that which is bad. Therefore, the usuliyun agree that the commanded action must be good and the prohibited action must be bad. But the disagreement is whether the goodness of the commanded action and the badness of the prohibited action is essential (dhati) which can be known by reason, or not essential but rather legal (shar'i) whose goodness and badness can only be known through the Law. And the well-known meanings of goodness and badness are three. Among them, the place of dispute and disagreement is only one meaning. Therefore, the author intended to mention these three meanings, specify the point of dispute, and then mention the statements and proofs of each party. He said: 'There is no dispute that...' The gist of the phrase mentioned by the author is that goodness and badness have three meanings: (1) Goodness as a quality of perfection...
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is a definition and dispute-map for the concepts of husn (good) and qubh (bad). The commentator connects this to the previous discussion: since Allah is the sole lawgiver, His commands are good and His prohibitions are bad by necessity. All usul scholars agree that commanded actions are good and prohibited actions are bad. The dispute is whether this goodness/badness is intrinsic (known by reason) or legal (known only through revelation). The commentator then introduces that there are three well-known meanings of husn and qubh, and the dispute pertains only to one of them. He begins to list the first meaning: 'Husn as a quality of perfection' but the text cuts off before completing the three. The commentary aims to isolate the exact point of disagreement between rationalists (like the Mu'tazila) and legalists (Hanafis/Ash'aris).

Key Terms
حسن (husn): One of three meanings: (1) perfection (kamal), (2) conformity to purpose, (3) being a source of praise in this world. The disputed meaning is the third.
قبيح (qubh): Opposite of husn; deficiency, being blameworthy. Three corresponding meanings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, how many meanings of 'husn' and 'qubh' are there, and how many are disputed?
A) Three meanings; all three are disputed
B) Three meanings; only one is disputed
C) Two meanings; both are disputed
D) One meaning; it is undisputed

Key Takeaway
The dispute over husn and qubh is limited to whether actions can be known as praiseworthy or blameworthy in the sense of divine reward/punishment by reason alone; the other two meanings (perfection and conformity to purpose) are agreed upon.

The Three Meanings of Good and Evil (Hasan wa Qabih) in Hanafi Usul
Defining the three senses of good and evil in Usul al-Fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three definitions of hasan and qabih in Hanafi usul, distinguish between the rational and shar'i meanings, and state which meaning is the locus of dispute between the Hanafi/Maturidi school and the Mu'tazila.

Core Idea
Only the third meaning of good (deserving praise/reward) is disputed — the first two are rational and undisputed across all schools.

In this passage from Sharh Muslim al-Thubut, the commentator explains that while all usul scholars agree that commanded acts are good and prohibited acts are evil, there is disagreement on why they are good or evil. The passage clarifies that 'good' and 'evil' have three distinct meanings, and only one of them is the battleground for the longstanding debate between the Ash'ari/Maturidi school and the Mu'tazila. Understanding this distinction is foundational for grasping the Hanafi position on the role of reason versus revelation in moral judgment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Three Definitions
Extract from the passage the three meanings of hasan and qabih: (1) perfection (sifat kamal) vs. deficiency (sifat nuqsan); (2) conformity to worldly benefit (maslahah) vs. opposition (mafsadah); (3) deserving of praise/blame in this world and reward/punishment in the Hereafter.
The author lists multiple scholars who have stated these meanings, showing their acceptance.
2
Recognize the Undisputed Meanings
The passage explicitly states that there is no disagreement that the first two meanings are rational (عقلی). All rational people recognize knowledge as good and ignorance as bad; similarly, pursuing worldly benefit and avoiding harm is universally understood. Their goodness/evil does not depend on revelation.
These two meanings are considered ‘aqli (rational) and qati (certain).
3
Locate the Disputed Meaning
The third meaning — whether an action is deserving of praise/reward or blame/punishment — is the sole locus of dispute. The Hanafi school (following Maturidi/Ash'ari theology) holds that this moral value is determined by Shari'ah alone; reason cannot independently obligate reward or punishment.
The Mu'tazila hold that actions are intrinsically good or evil in this sense and reason can know them.
4
Apply the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Consider a person who never received revelation. According to the Mu'tazila, they are accountable for knowing good and evil by reason (e.g., that lying is blameworthy). According to Hanafis, without revelation, the third meaning does not apply — they are not liable for reward or punishment based on rational judgment alone.
This practical difference highlights the importance of the dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مگر اللہ سے ، اس معنی کی بنا پر معتزلہ کا رد ہو گا جنہوں نے بندہ کو افعال کا خالق مانکر اہل حق کی مخالفت کی ہے۔ یہ بھی ہو سکتا ہے کہ حکم سے حکم قضاء وقد ر مراد ہو، اس تقدیر پر معنی یہ ہوگا کہ قضاء وقد نہیں ہے مگر اللہ کا، چنانچہ الہ عز شانہ کے علم قدیم میں ہر حکم مقدر ہو چکا ہے اور ہرشی کا فیصلہ ہو چکا ہے۔ لہذا اب کسی کا حکم نہیں اس تقدیر پر جنسی حکم کی نفی غیر اللہ سے صحیح ہوگی گو کہ بعض کفار و ظالمین کا حکم بظاہر حکم شرع کے مخالف معلوم ہوتا ہے تاہم وہ بھی حکم قضاء و قدر کے موافق ہے البتہ معتزلہ بندے کے افعال اختیار یہ میں قضاء وقدر سے انکار کرتے ہیں اس لئے کہ ان کے نزدیک بندہ افعال کا حاکم ہے۔ یہ بھی احتمال ہے کہ حکم سے حکم تکلیف نہ مراد ہو بلکہ حکم آخرت مراد ہو، اس صورت میں یہ حکم با نفاق کل عز شانہ کے ساتھ مختص ہے جیسا کہ بادشاہت مختص ہوگی ارشاد ہے لمن الملك اليوم لله الواحد القهار، یاد رہے کہ حکم کے اس معنی کا اختصاص جناب باری تعالیٰ اسمہ کے ساتھ ہونے میں امت اجابت میں سے کسی کا اختلاف نہیں ہے۔
قوله لانزاع فى أن الفعل حسن وقبيح عقلا
جب یہ بات معلوم ہوگئی کہ پوری امت مسلمہ کے نزدیک حاکم مطلق صرف اور صرف اللہ تعالیٰ ہے تو اس سے یہ بھی معلوم ہو گیا کہ آمروناهی در حقیقت صرف اللہ ہی ہے پھر یہ ظاہر ہے کہ آمر ونا ہی جس مامور بہ کا حکم دے گا وہ حسن ہوگا اور جس منہی عنہ سے منع کرے گا وہ تیج ہوگا کیونکہ آمرونا ہی اللہ تعالیٰ ہے اور وہ حاکم مطلق ہے اور حکیم نہیں حکم دیتا ہے مگر اسی بات کا جو حسن ہو اور نہیں منع کرتا ہے مگر اس بات سے جو تیج ہو اس لئے اصولین کا اس امر پہ اتفاق ہے کہ مامور بہ کا حسن ہونا اور منھی عنہ کا فتیح ہونا ضروری ہے مگر اختلاف اس میں ہے کہ مامور بہ کا حسن اور منہی عنہ کا فتح کیا لذاتہ ہے جسکو بذریعہ عقل اور اک کیا جا سکتا ہے یا لذاتہ نہیں ہے بلکہ شرعی ہے جس کا حسن و فتح بہت شریعت سے معلوم ہو سکتا ہے اور حسن و فتح کے معنی مشہورہ تین ہیں جن میں محل نزاع و اختلاف صرف ایک معنی ہے اس لئے مصنف نے ان تینوں معانی کو بیان کر کے محل نزاع متعین کرتے ہوئے ہر فریق کے اقوال و دلائل کو ذکر کرنے کا قصد کیا تو کہا لا نزاع في أن الخ۔
ماتن کی ذکر کردہ عبارت کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ حسن و فتح کے تین معانی ہیں (۱) حسن صفت کمال اور فتح صفت نقصان کے معنی میں ہے کہا جاتا ہے اعلم حسن یعنی العلم صفة الكمال، الجيل قبیح یعنی الجھل صفہ نقصان ۔ چنانچہ امام نے محصل میں، بیضاوی نے منہاج میں اسنوی نے شرح منہاج میں، صدر الشریعہ نے توضیح میں تفتازانی نے شرح مقاصد میں اور ابن الہمام وغیرھم نے اپنی اپنی کتابوں میں اس کی تصریح کی ہے۔
(۲) حسن بغرض دنیاوی کے موافق ہونے اور فتح غرض دنیاوی کے مخالف ہونے کے معنی میں ہے، جس میں حسن کو مصلحت سے اور فتح کو مفسدہ سے تعبیر کیا جاتا ہے کہا جاتا ہے موافقة السلطان الظالم حسن اى ملائم للغرض الدنياوى و مخالفته قبیح اى منافر ومخالف للغرض الدنياوى یعنی ظالم بادشاہ کی ہاں حضوری حسن ہے یعنی غرض دنیاوی کے موافق ہے اور اسکی مخالفت قبیح ہے یعنی غرض دنیاوی کے مخالف ہے، چنانچہ امام آمدی نے احکام میں ، ابن حاجب نے مختصر میں قاضی عضد نے شرح المختصر والمواقف میں اور تفتازانی نے شرح مقاصد میں اور ابن الہمام وغیرہ نے اپنی اپنی کتابوں میں تصریح کی ہے۔
(۳) حسن و قبیح کا تیسرا معنی یہ ہے کہ دنیا میں کسی فعل کے کرنے نہ کرنے پر اللہ تعالیٰ کی طرف سے مدح وزم کا مستحق ہونا اور آخرت میں اسی کام کے سبب ثواب و عذاب کا مستحق ہونا ہے پس دنیا میں جس کام کے فعل و ترک پر مدح اور آخرت میں ثواب ہو حسن ہے اور دنیا میں جس کام کے فعل و ترک پر ذم اور آخرت میں عذاب ہو وہ قبیح ہے۔
حسن و قبیح کے تینوں معانی ذہن نشین کر لینے کے بعد یہ یادر ہے کہ پہلے دونوں معنوں کے عقلی ہونے میں کسی کا اختلاف نہیں چنانچہ علم کا صفت کمال ہونا، جہل کا صفت نقصان ہونا عقلا ثابت ہے اور ظالم بادشاہ کے ہاں میں ہاں کرنا، اس کی مخالفت سے گریز کرنا غرض دنیاوی کے موافق اور مصلحت کے عین مطابق ہے اسی طرح اس کی مخالفت کرنا غرض دنیاوی کے خلاف اور مفسدہ کو دعوت دینا ہے اور یہ عقلی ہے کہ اول کے حسن اور ثانی کے فتح کو جمیع عقلاء ادراک کرتے ہیں درود شرع پر اسکا حسن و قبح موقوف نہیں ہے۔ ہاں دونوں معانی بھی مواضع ، اوقات اور اشخاص کے اعتبار سے کبھی مختلف ہو جاتے ہیں جیسے کہ احسان ایک صفت کمال ہے دل کو بھاتا ہے اور اغراض دنیاوی کے موافق بھی ہے
But from Allah. Based on this meaning, the Mu'tazila will be refuted, who opposed the people of truth by considering the servant as the creator of actions. It is also possible that by 'hukm' is meant the decree of divine predestination; on this assumption the meaning would be that there is no decree except from Allah. Accordingly, in Allah's eternal knowledge, every decree is destined and every matter is decided. Hence now no one has any decree. On this assumption, the negation of every decree from other than Allah would be correct, even though some decrees of disbelievers and oppressors apparently contradict the Shari'ah decree; nevertheless they are in accordance with the decree of predestination. However, the Mu'tazila deny predestination in the voluntary actions of the servant because according to them the servant is the determiner of actions. It is also possible that by 'hukm' is meant the legal obligation (taklif) rather than the decree of the afterlife; in that case this hukm is exclusively with Allah, just as sovereignty is exclusive. He said: 'To whom belongs the dominion today? To Allah, the One, the Subduer.' Remember that there is no disagreement among the community of acceptance regarding the exclusivity of this meaning of hukm with Allah Almighty. 
His saying: 'There is no dispute that the action is good and evil rationally.' When it became known that according to the entire Muslim community the absolute Determiner is only Allah, then it also became known that the commander and prohibitor is in reality only Allah. Then it is obvious that the thing commanded by the commander and prohibitor will be good (hasan) and the thing prohibited will be evil (qabih), because the commander and prohibitor is Allah, and He is the absolute Determiner, and the Wise does not command except that which is good, and does not prohibit except that which is evil. Therefore the usul scholars agree that it is necessary for the commanded thing to be good and the prohibited thing to be evil. But the disagreement is whether the goodness of the commanded thing and the evil of the prohibited thing is intrinsic (li dhatihi), which can be known through reason, or not intrinsic but rather shar'i, whose goodness and evil can only be known through the Shari'ah. And the well-known meanings of good and evil are three, among which the locus of dispute and disagreement is only one meaning. Therefore the author, intending to mention the statements and proofs of each party after defining these three meanings and specifying the locus of dispute, said: 'There is no dispute that...' 
The gist of the text's mentioned statement is that good and evil have three meanings: (1) Good in the sense of perfection (sifat kamal) and evil in the sense of deficiency (sifat nuqsan). It is said: 'Knowledge is good,' meaning knowledge is a perfection; 'Ignorance is evil,' meaning ignorance is a deficiency. Accordingly, Imam [Razi] in al-Muhassal, Baydawi in al-Minhaj, Isnawi in Sharh al-Minhaj, Sadr al-Shari'ah in al-Tawdih, Taftazani in Sharh al-Maqasid, Ibn al-Humam and others in their books have stated this. (2) Good in the sense of being in accordance with worldly benefit, and evil in the sense of being opposed to worldly benefit. This is expressed as good being maslahah (benefit) and evil being mafsadah (corruption). It is said: 'Agreeing with an oppressive sultan is good,' i.e., suitable for worldly benefit, 'and opposing him is evil,' i.e., repugnant and opposed to worldly benefit. So agreeing with an oppressive king is good because it suits worldly benefit, and opposing him is evil because it opposes worldly benefit. Accordingly, Imam al-Amidi in al-Ahkam, Ibn al-Hajib in al-Mukhtasar, Qadi 'Adud in Sharh al-Mukhtasar wa al-Mawaqif, Taftazani in Sharh al-Maqasid, Ibn al-Humam and others in their books have stated this. (3) The third meaning of good and evil is that in this world, an action deserves praise or blame from Allah for doing or not doing it, and in the Hereafter, it deserves reward or punishment due to that action. So whatever action, by doing or leaving it, brings praise in this world and reward in the Hereafter is good; and whatever action, by doing or leaving it, brings blame in this world and punishment in the Hereafter is evil. 
After memorizing the three meanings of good and evil, remember that there is no disagreement that the first two meanings are rational. Thus, that knowledge is a perfection and ignorance is a deficiency is rationally established. And agreeing with an oppressive king, avoiding opposing him, is in accordance with worldly benefit and exactly in line with maslahah; similarly opposing him is against worldly benefit and invites mafsadah. And it is rational that all rational people perceive the goodness of the former and the evil of the latter; its goodness and evil do not depend on the Shari'ah. Yes, the two meanings also sometimes differ according to places, times, and persons, such as benevolence (ihsan) which is a perfection that pleases the heart and is also in accordance with worldly benefits.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is an extended commentary (sharh) on the matn of Muslim al-Thubut, explaining the three established meanings of 'good' (hasan) and 'evil' (qabih) in usul al-fiqh. The commentator begins by concluding the earlier discussion on the exclusivity of divine sovereignty and predestination, refuting the Mu'tazila who affirm human creation of actions. Then the commentator transitions to the core topic: there is no dispute that actions are good or evil rationally (لا نزاع في أن الفعل حسن وقبيح عقلا). This phrase from the matn is explained by noting that all usul scholars agree that the commanded act must be good (hasan) and the prohibited act must be evil (qabih). The only disagreement is whether this goodness or evil is intrinsic (li dhatihi) and knowable by reason, or contingent on divine command (shar'i) and only knowable through revelation. The commentator then defines the three meanings of hasan and qabih: (1) perfection vs. deficiency — e.g., knowledge is a perfection (sifat kamal), ignorance is a deficiency (sifat nuqsan). (2) Conformity vs. opposition to worldly benefit — e.g., agreeing with an oppressive sultan is hasan because it serves worldly benefit, opposing him is qabih because it brings harm. (3) Deserving of praise/blame in this world and reward/punishment in the Hereafter due to the act itself. The commentator stresses that the first two meanings are undisputed: all rational people recognize them, and they do not depend on revelation. The third meaning is the locus of dispute between the Ash'arites (who hold that good/evil are determined by Shari'ah) and the Mu'tazilites (who hold they are intrinsic and knowable by reason). The passage also lists major scholars who have mentioned these meanings, showing the consensus on the first two.

Key Terms
حسن (hasan): In this passage, hasan has three meanings: (1) perfection (sifat kamal), (2) conformity to worldly benefit, (3) deserving praise/reward.
قبیح (qabih): The opposite of hasan: (1) deficiency, (2) opposition to worldly benefit, (3) deserving blame/punishment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the three meanings of hasan and qabih is the locus of disagreement between the Ash'ari/Maturidi school and the Mu'tazila?
A) The first meaning: perfection vs. deficiency
B) The second meaning: conformity vs. opposition to worldly benefit
C) The third meaning: deserving praise/reward vs. blame/punishment
D) All three meanings are disputed

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school agrees that actions have rational goodness (perfection and worldly benefit) but holds that moral or theological worth (praise/blame, reward/punishment) depends on divine revelation.

Definitions of Rational and Intrinsic Good and Evil in Hanafi Usul
Defining 'aqli and dhati with justifications and a critical objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the definitions of 'aqli and dhati good/evil, explain the objection to one reason for 'aqli, and distinguish the Hanafi-Maturidi position from the Mu'tazila.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, goodness and badness are known by reason but do not impose a ruling without revelation.

This passage from the Sharh Muslim al-Thubut defines two foundational terms in the Hanafi discussion of husn wa qubh (good and evil): 'aqli (rational) and dhati (intrinsic). Understanding these terms is crucial for grasping the Hanafi-Maturidi position that reason can perceive value but does not create legal obligation. The author presents multiple reasons for each term and then critiques one of them, showing the precision of Hanafi usul reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term 'aqli
Locate where the author explains why an act's goodness or badness is called 'aqli. Note the three reasons given.
The reasons are: (1) perception by reason, (2) uncaused nature, (3) doer deserves praise/blame by reason.
2
Analyze the objection to the third reason
Read the objection carefully: it claims the third reason wrongly implies reason perceives all acts. Understand why this is a problem for the third reason.
The objection states reason only perceives some acts, not all.
3
Define dhati (intrinsic)
Now read the three reasons for calling goodness/badness dhati. Note that they either inhere in the essence, attach to accidents, or are not imposed by the Lawgiver.
The term dhati contrasts with shar'i (revealed).
4
Connect to the Madhhab dispute
Relate these definitions to the broader Hanafi-Maturidi vs Mu'tazila disagreement on rational good and evil.
Hanafis: reason perceives but does not obligate. Mu'tazila: reason obligates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
یعنی اسکو عقلی اس لئے کہتے ہیں کہ کبھی اسکا حسن و قبح عقل سے ادراک کیا جاتا ہے یا اس لئے کہ جب اسکا ثبوت بلا جعل جاعل ہے تو اس کی شان یہ ہے اسکو عقل سے ادراک کیا جائے اور کہا گیا کہ اس کا عقلی نام اس لئے رکھا گیا کہ فعل حسن اور فعل قبیح کا فاعل عقل کے نزدیک مدح وذم کا مستحق ہوتا ہے لیکن اس میں اعتراض ہے۔
اعتراض: عقلی ہونے کی تیسری وجہ تسمیہ پر ماتن کو اعتراض ہے اور وہ یہ ہے کہ وجہ تسمیہ سے سمجھا جاتا ہے کہ مدح و ذم کے استحقاق کا مدرک تمام مادوں میں عقل ہے حالانکہ ایسا نہیں کیونکہ عقل صرف بعض مادہ میں مدرک ہے۔
بلفظ دیگر وجہ تسمیہ کی دلالت اس پر ہو رہی ہے کہ کل فعل کا فاعل عند العقل مدح وذم کا مستحق ہے حالانکہ ایسا لازم نہیں ہے بلکہ لازم ایجاب جزئی ہے کیونکہ عقل کو جمیع افعال کے فاعل کے بارے میں استحقاق مدح و ذم کا علم نہیں البتہ بعض کا ہے لہذا قول ثالث تام نہیں۔
ذاتی کے وجوہ: ذاتی کہنے کی کئی وجہیں ہیں۔ (1) بلا اعتبار شرع ہر فعل میں بذاتہ جہت حسنة وجہت قبیحة ہوتی ہے پس اس لئے کہ ذات فعل میں حسن و قبح داخل ہے اسکو ذاتی کہتے ہیں کہ داخل ذات ذاتی کہلاتا ہے۔ (۲) کبھی ذات فعل میں حسن و قبح نہیں ہوتا بلکہ فعل کے عوارض میں ہوتا ہے اس لئے معروض کی طرف نسبت کرتے ہوئے ذاتی کہہ دیتے ہیں۔ (۳) اس لئے کہ جب اسکا حسن و قبح جعل شارع سے ثابت نہیں ہے تو ظاہر ہے کہ اس کی استناد ذات فعل کی طرف ہوگی۔
That is, it is called 'rational' because sometimes its goodness and badness are perceived by reason, or because when its establishment is without a maker's making, its characteristic is that it should be perceived by reason. And it was said that it was named rational because the doer of a good or bad act is deserving of praise and blame in reason's view. But there is an objection to this.
Objection: The author has an objection to the third reason for naming it 'rational'; that is, from the reason for naming it is understood that the perceiver of deserving praise and blame in all matters is reason, but it is not so because reason perceives only some matters.
In other words, the reason for naming indicates that the doer of every act deserves praise and blame according to reason, but this is not necessary; rather it is a partial necessary implication because reason does not know the deserving of praise and blame for all acts, only for some. Therefore the third opinion is not complete.
Reasons for 'intrinsic': There are several reasons for calling it 'intrinsic'.
(1) Without considering the law, every act in itself has a good aspect and a bad aspect. So because goodness and badness enter into the essence of the act, it is called intrinsic, since what enters the essence is called intrinsic.
(2) Sometimes goodness and badness are not in the essence of the act but in its accidents, so by attributing to the subject it is called intrinsic.
(3) Because when its goodness and badness are not established by the maker's (Lawgiver's) making, it is evident that its attribution will be to the essence of the act.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage defines two key terms in the discussion of good and evil (ḥusn wa qubḥ) in the Hanafi uṣūl tradition: 'aqli (rational) and dhātī (intrinsic). The author first explains that an act's goodness or badness is called 'aqli when it is perceived by reason alone, without needing revelation. He reports three possible reasons for this terminology: either because the perception is by reason, or because the act's existence is uncaused and thus known by reason, or because the doer deserves praise or blame by rational judgment. The author then raises an objection to the third reason: it wrongly implies that reason knows the moral value of all acts, whereas in fact reason only knows some. Then he gives three reasons for calling an act's goodness or badness dhātī: (1) goodness and badness inhere in the act's essence, (2) they attach to accidents but are attributed to the act, or (3) they are not imposed by the Lawgiver and thus stem from the act itself. The distinction sets up the Hanafi Maturidi view that reason perceives good and bad but does not impose a ruling without revelation.

Key Terms
العقلی (al-ʿaqlī): That whose goodness or badness is perceived by reason alone, without revelation.
الذاتی (al-dhātī): Goodness or badness that inheres in the essence of the act or its accidents, not imposed by revelation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the author object to the third reason for calling good and evil 'aqli?
A) Reason perceives all acts equally.
B) Reason perceives only some acts, not all.
C) Reason never perceives good or evil.
D) Revelation overrides reason completely.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, 'aqli good and evil are perceived by reason but do not impose a legal ruling without revelation; they are also called dhātī because they inhere in the act's nature or attributes.
